SCHOONER SIHKS
IN LAKE ONTARIO
Captain the Only Person
Rescued.
AT LEAST EIGHT LIVES LOST
SKIPPER'S WIFE AMONG THE
DROWNED.
The Three-Master St. Peter Goes
Down Off Sodus Point in Sight
of a Tug Sent to Her
Rescue.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ROCHESTER, N. V., Oct. 27.â A
special to the Herald from Sodus says:
The three-masted schooner St. Peter, of
St. Vincent, sank this afternoon about
five miles northwest of Sodus, on Lake
Ontario, with all on board, save Cap
tain John D. Griffin, who was rescued
in a precarious condition.
The schooner showed signs of dis
tress early this morning as she drifted
past Charlotte. During the forenoon
the people of Pultneyville sighted her
and immediately telegraphed to Sodus
Feint asking that help be offered. The
tug Cornelia started for the rescue, but
the great seas nearly swamped the boat
and the Cornelia was compelled to re
turn to the harbor. Word was sent to
Charlotte that the distressed vessel had
been sighted near Pultneyville, and the
tug Proctor started with the life
saving crew. When within a mile of
the St. Peter the crew on the Proctor
were horrified to see the ship sink.
In ten minutes the tug was cruising
about the spot where she went down.
Captain Griffin was picked up in an un
conscious condition. After spending a
half-hour looking for the other mem
bers of the crew the tug started for
Sodus Point, where medical assistance
was secured for the captain. He is
still unconscious, but will recover.
The wife of the captain was lost, as
was Mate McLaren of Kingston. At
least eight persons perished.
Olen Jolly and Jacob Serburg o-f this
town were standing on the shore when
the St. Peter went down and declare
the boat was not more than a half-mile
from the shore. It is thought that the
St. Peter lost her rudder and that the
heavy seas caused her to spring aleak.
The St. Peter was bound from Oswego
to Toledo with 700 tons of coal.
LOUISVILLE DEFAULTER
CAPTURED IN LONDON
Charles G. Henning, Who Absconded
With $8000 Bank Funds,
Under Arrest.
LOUISVILLE, Oct. 27.â Chief of Police
Haager to-day received a notification that
Charles G. Henning. who absconded from
the Bank of Louisville with $8000 several
weeks ago, had been captured in London,
England.
Young Henning bore a name well
known and honored throughout Ken
tucky and the adjoining States. He is
the son of John W. Henning, who for
many years prior to his death, soms
twelve years ago, made up the celebrated
firm of Hennrng & Speed, real estate
dealers and capitalists. Joshua Speed,
the junior partner, was a brother of James
Sp-ed, President Lincoln's Attorney Gen
eral, and whose warm and intimate
friendship for the martyred President
dated from very early days when, as a
young storekeeper in Springfield, 111., he
first met Air. Lincoln. This connection j
with the President gave the firm great
influence at a time when much money
was to be made in handling real estate.
A; Mr. Henning's death he left each of
his two sons some $350,000 and his interest j
in the business. Charles Henning, with i
his brother, was largely interested in the
reorganization of the several lines of
street railways in New Orleans Into one
general concern known as the New Or
leans Traction Company. The enterprise
was not a financial success and young
Henning, with se%eral others, is said to
have lost the greater part, if not the
whole of his fortune. His family connec
tions easily secured Tilm a position of re
muneration and trust in the Bank
of Louisville, of which his father
had for thirty years been a di
rector, and he was generally thought
to be retrieving his losses, when
j a short time ago the news of his ab- |
sconding with $8000 of the bank's funds j
! fell like a thunderbolt on the community, j
WEED BELIEVED TO
HEAD A SYNDICATE
May Be Working to Obtain a Foot
hold in the San Domingo
Republic.
WASHINGTON, Oct. 27.-Careful in
quiry at the Navy Department demon
strates the absolutely unofficial maracter
of any commission such as that said to be
headed by Smith M. Weed, which was re
ported recently at Nassau, B. W. 1., In j
conference with President Heureaux of j
San Domingo. It is said that Captain Con- j
verse of the Montgomery may have taken j
Mr. Weed on his ship as a guest. The i
Montgomery has been ordered from New
port to Charleston to participate in the
peace jubilee In the latter city, and Cap- j
tain Converse obtained permission to j
touch at Nassau en route.
It is believed here that Mr. Weed Is the !
representative of an American syndicate i
which has agreed to administer most of
the affairs of the Dominican Government,
collecting customs receipts in return and
paying a large sum yearly to the of
ticials of the island. Some such scheme as j
this has been opfrated by an American
syndicate In Honduras for several years !
with marked success, it is asserted, and j
this may have attracted the attention of I
speculators to the possibilities of other |
Central American and West Indian coun-
tries.
So far as any proposition to buy or ac
quire Samana Bay as a United States
coaling station is concerned, naval au
thorities declare there is no truth in the
story. The Samana Bay project was a
favorite of President Grant, but has not
taken serious shape since, and is less pos
sible of consummation now that the
United States has splendid facilities of
Its own in the matter of coaling stations
on the island of Porto Rico.
JUSTICE FIELD IS
NOT AT DEATH'S DOOR
His Sister Denies Recent Reports
Regarding the Alarming Con
dition of the Jurist.
WASHINGTON, Oct. 27.â A representa
tive of The Call who visited the residence
of Justice Field to-day was told by the
sister of the aged jurist that there was
absolutely no foundation for the alarm
ing reports printed in San Francisco con*
cernlng his condition.
"He is, of course, very feeble," said his
sister, "and receives no visitors, but this
has been the case for a year, and there
has been no change in his condition to
warrant such reports. Justice Field sits
up, reads and takes his sleep and meals
the same as usual. Mrs. Field is not
well, and it may be that this fact gave
rise to the story that my brother is vory
ill."

THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, OCTOBER 28, 1893.
LEGISLATIVE
TICKET WILL
BE ELECTED
Hon. F. M. Leavitt Is
Warmly Received.
REPUBLICANS ARE POSITIVE
ALLIANCE PROGRAMME FOR A
BUSY WEEK.
Will Participate at Meetings, Ban
quets and Parades on Both
bides of the
Say.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
90S Broadway, Oct. 27.
Two years ago the Republican legisla
tive ticket was elected in this county
without a break. On that occasion one
Senator and six Assemblymen were elect
ed. At the coming election two Senators
and six Assemblymen are to be elected,
and there is no doubt but that the Re
publican ticket will be entirely success
ful.
One of the most active campaigners for
the legislative ticket is Frank A. Leav
itt, who two years ago was elected to the
Assembly and made such a good record
that when the late Eli Denison declared
his intention, long before his death, of
quitting politics, it was regarded as fit
ting and proper that his Senatorial mantle
should fall on the shoulders of Assembly
man Leavitt. Mr. Leavitt has recently
gone all over his district and his con
fidence in the success of the Republican
ticket has been materially strengthened
by his experiences with the voters. He
has also canvassed the situation in re
gard to the Assembly candidates and says
there is no room for doubt as to the re
sult. At the many meetings which he
attended Assemblyman Leavitt was very
enthusiastically received and assurances
of support were given him by many who
are utterly disgusted with the peculiar
antics of the so-called fusionists.
President Lukens of the Republican Al
liance has Issued the following orders:
Saturday, October 29â The Alliance muÃt turn
out at home. Hon. Henry T. Gage will be In
Oakland at the tabernacle. Report at head
quarters at 7 o'clock sharp. There will be a
supper served at headquarters after the meet
ing, at which the Union League Drill Corps
will be present.
Monday, October 31â The Alliance goes to San
Francisco. The State Central Committee has
given the Alliance a special Invitation to pa
rade that night, and the Union League will
entertain the Alliance at their club rooms in
the Palace Hotel after the parade. This is to
be San Francisco's big demonstration.
Friday, November 4â The Alliance is Invited
to escort Hon. Victor H. Metcalf from Fruit
vale Station out Fruitvale avenue to a meeting
arranged by East Oakland and Fruitvale Re
publicans. After the meeting the Alliance will
be given a bullshead supper at Fruitvale.
Saturday, November sâwill5 â will be an Alliance
night at the Macdonough Theater. Hon.
George C. Perkins and Hon. Victor H. Metcalf
will be the speakers. An Alliance supper will
be given our Congressional candidate and
United States Senator after the meeting, at
which honorary members of the Alliance will
be invited.
Let every member of the Alliance go to San
Francisco Monday next, October 31, on the 6:45
p. m. train. The 7 o'clock will get you there

ROUMANIAN NOBLEMAN
ARRESTED IN CHICAGO
Claims He Barely Escaped Becoming
the Victim of Porkvihe
Sharpers.
CHICAGO, Oct. 27.â Count Leopold de
Bovitlz of Bucharest, Roumania, was ar
rested to-day on a charge of obtaining
$8 75 by false pretenses. The Count
claims that he is a victim of a clever
confidence game In which it was Intended
that he should be swindled out of $6000.
As a result of his suspicions he caused
the arrest of Marvin Victor Henshaw,
teacher of dramatic art, and L. E. Hen
shaw and D. J. Hallaman, lawyers.
The Count came to Chicago several days
ago upon ihe solicitation, he Bays, of
Ernest Salvator, a playwright. He be
came Interested in a play written by Sal
vator, and was introduced to Martin Vic
tor Henshaw. Henshaw, according to the
Count, agreed to stage the play if Bovitlz
would furnish the money. The Count did
not have any ready money, but volun
teered to send to Roumania for the nec
essary amount. Henshaw gave him $8 75
with which to pay for the cablegram, and
when the money was not forthcoming a
warrant was obtained for the nobleman's
arrest. Bovitiz was arraigned in the
Criminal Court to-day, but the hearing
was continued until November. Mr. Hen
shaw and the two lawyers deny that any
attempt was made to defraud the Count.
WOOD REPLIES TO
THE FRENCH PROTEST
Too Many Paupers at Santiago Al
ready, Necessitating Prohib
itory Measures.
SANTIAGO DE CUBA, Oct. 27.â General
Wood left here to-day on board the dis
patch boat Hist, accompanied by his aide
camp and clerical staff, for the purpose
of viÃiting Manzanillo and to Instruct
Colonel Pettit to arrange the conduct of
the civil business there.
The French Consul here has written to
General Wood protesting strongly against
the order prohibiting the landing of ne
groes from Hayti. General Wood replied
that he could not allow any more paupers
here, as there were too many of them.
General Mayla Rodriguez, the Insur
gent commander, visited General "Wood at
the palace this morning, he was accom
panied by Colonel Garcia, who acted as
an interpreter, and remained over an
hour He said he was immensely pleased
v/ith the manner in which things are
conducted here and congratulated General
Wood on the work he had accomplished.
General Wood visited the San Carlos
Club laÃt night at the special invitat on
of the committee to add to the reception
given to General Rodriguez.
MARRIES HIS WIFE.
Slater Was Not Sure the First Knot
Was Tied Securely. â¢
SAN RAFAEL. Oct. 27.-" Well, I guess
we were married before all right enough,
but my wife's and my relatives made so
much ado over court decisions and one
thing and r another ; that we decided to
have the knot tied in conventional style."
This was the rather peculiar explana
tion given to-day by James Slater, a ship
joiner employed by the Union Iron Works,
after he had been united to Mrs. Anna B.
Pierce of 537 Alvarado street, by Justice
Gardner. According to Slater, both were
divorced persons when they wished to
marry a year ago, but Slater had not
been divorced a year. â â¢â  In order to avoid
the law providing that a divorced person
could not marry within a year after the
decree was granted, they ; went- to Monte
rey and were married at sea by Rev.
George Snodgrass. Their relatives made
such a protest that they finally concluded
to be united in "conventional" style;
hence to-day's ceremony. ,~" -; j-r

Crushed by Mill Machinery.
CAHTO, Oct. 27.â Charles Britt, a re
cent arrival from Rocky Ford, Colo., met
a frightful death to-day in the Layton
ville Flour Mills. Britt was caught in the
main shaft, his lower limbs being ground
to a pulp in the machinery. He lived ten
hours. Britt waa 22 years of age and
unmarried.
"The Dead Body," by H. G. Wells,
author of ' The War of the Worlds,"
in next Sunday's Call.
THE DEATH OF
POPULISM IS
ACCOMPLISHED
Strangled by Demo
cratic Corruption.
EDITORIAL FROM "INDUSTRY"
A CURSE ON BRIBE - TAKING
OFFICE SEEKERS.
People's Party Was Sold Out at Sac
ramento by Those Who Sought
for Personal Ad
vancement.
The following is an editorial from
"Industry," the leading Populist paper
of California, published at Oakland,
October 27, 1898:
POPULISM DIES IN THE CESSPOOL
OF DEMOCRATIC CORRUPTION.
After the fusion convention at Sacra
mento, the chairman and secretary and
fifty-seven members withdrew from the
San Francisco People's Party County
Committee, leaving It in possession of A.
B. Kinne, George Silvey and other Demo
cratic office-holders. One of the Gal
laghers openly boasted to a member of
the committee that he owned the commit
tee and had paid for it. Money was of
fered openly for votes in the interest of a
fusion delegation at the election of the
delegates to Sacramento, and the fare
and expenses of most of the fusion dele
gates were paid with Democratic money.
After the Democratic ring got Maguire
nominated it completely ignored what re
mained of the Populist County Commit
tee in San Francisco. The Phelan Demo
cratic convention met and nominated a
full ticket of Democrats, never noticing
the existence of the Populist Piece Club
run by Kinne, Silvey & Co.; not a Popu
list was nominated by the Democrats.
Then the Populist fusion Piece Club re
solved itself into a convention and put up
Its indorsements for sale, indorsing both
Democrats and Republicans in a dis
gusting, loathsome manner. Men were
taken into secret session before terms
were arranged. The price was small, but
more than the indorsement of such a
piece club was worth. Not a straight
Populist was nominated. Carroll, a sore
head Democrat who failed of a Demo
cratic nomination, was given a nomina
tion for Justice of the Peace, and H. W.
Philbrook, who ran in 1896 as candidate
of the A. P. A. party, was nominated
and will get less votes than he got when
he ran as an A. P. A. alone. These, we be
lieve, are the only ones not nominees of
other parties.
On Thursday night they went through
the farce of a ratification meeting. The
first speaker was Phelan, an open gold
bug, followed by several Democratic
nominees. Not a Popuiist speech was
made or a Populist heard of.
So expires in the cesspool of rotten poli
tics the party which once commanded
honor and respect. Yet bribe-taking can
didates on the fusion ticketâ bribed by
officeâ and their organs have the gall to
denounce the men who have spen t years
to build up the People's party, simply be
cause they have tried to preserve It and
kee~ it from being strangled to death in
this loathsome manner. May an all-mer
ciful God forgive them. If He does it will
not be because they know not what they
do. They sold out at Sacramento for the
promise of office and salaries and went
back to their homes to receive the nom
inations for local offices, which was the
price of their votes. Such fusion should
be defeated by voting directly against it.
SMOKE DID NOT
OBSCURE THE FUN
POLITICS AND MIRTH AT NA
TIVE SONS' HALL.
Rincon Parlor Entertains and Is En
tertained by the Wit, Humor
and Talent of Its Mem
bers.
It was a "smoKer" Rincon Parlor No.
72, N. S. Q. W-, gave in Shasta Hall, Na
tive Sons' building Wednesday evening,
and it was the happiest gathering of
the season. The meeting was partly
social, but many aspirants for pub
lic office appeared during the evening
and were presented. They did not burden
the merry crowd with tales of politics ur
misconstructions of issues, however, but
joined in the fun. For instance, Charles
F. Kenneally, Republican and Citizens'
Republican nominee for the Assembly of
the Twenty-ninth District, quoted and
gave a few original essays far, far dis
tant from legislative matters, and Coro
ner Hawkins pulleu off his coat and "tore
off a few yards" on the piano that Chair
man Mogan stated would cause the dead
in the Morgue to seek other quarters if
played in that locality. The wit and tal
ent of the gathering was called upon to
do the entertaining, and in consequence it
was long after midnight before, the last
guest left the hall homeward bound.
A J Rocco opened the fun by deliver
ing an "essay" in Italian dialect, rich in
style, picturing Police Judge Mogan do
ing the neighborhood of the fish wharf
soliciting votes. He was promptly sen
tenced to serve a term of six months
twice a year by Mogan, and John Hoey.
candidate for Senator from the fc-i Knt ~
eenth District, was called upon to address
the audience. He pictured his hopes and
fears, and gave way to Edward Hanra
han who asked and stated that ne ex
pected the support of his comrades of
the parlor, which, he believed, would ma
terially aid him in his fight for the As
8 ChaVles Kenneall- who is confident that
he' will assist the next Legislature in its
labors, then took the floor and proceeded
to entertain the smokers. He was suc
cessful to such a degree that Mogan de
clared him elected without waiting tor
November 8. Joseph Hanson then sang a
song W. H. Growney made a ittle
speech, and D. E. Hanlon, in response to
a call, took the platform. Hanlon gave
Imitations of the efforts of the great
tragedians. Booth. Irving and Keane, and
the g ra vings of John McCullough. JUMH
H Barry. Finlay cook, Charles T. Con
lan Charles Myers, Justice of the Peace
Barry William A. Deane. Republican
candidate for County Clerk and Charles
Whelan were presented in turn and
played their part, and then Dr. Hawkins
rendered his "piano solo." Notwithstand
ing the solo the merriment went on, en
tertainers came to the front, speakers ap
peared until the rapping of the chair
man's gavel and a few brief words ended
the fun and all filed out to await the com
ing of another "smoker."
No Parade Needed.
The Republican parade committee held
a meeting Wednesday evening in the
Union League Club rooms at the Palace,
H. C. Flrebaugh presiding.
The report of the committee of five, ap
pointed at the last meeting to confer with :
the State Central and County commit
tees, resulted in a decision not to have
a parade this campaignâ it being the ]
sense of the meeting that the Republican
outlook was so favorable as not to re
quire any. It was thought best, how
ever, to preserve the organization. In the
hope that it might assist the State Cen
tral and County committees in watching
the polls. With this object In view, the
secretary was instructed to invite a rep
resentation from every Republican club
In this city to be present at the next
meeting, to be held at the samo place, on"
Tuesday evening next, at S o'clock.

By resolution the Society of Old Friends
has decided to complete the formulation
of the Old Friends' State and county tick
et at the next smoker, to be held No
vember 2, In Washington Hall.
PHELAN'S WAIL
ON THE SUPREME
COURT DECISION
It is Arraigned byGeo.
C. Sargent.
ISSUE WITH THE MAYOR
JUDGES MUST NOT BE SWAYED
BY THE PUBLIC.
An Answer to the Hefter's Cry
to ' Beat Two ! Judicial
Candidates for
Office.
George C. Sargent has this to say in
regard to the criticisms passed by Mr.
Phelan upon the Supreme Court:
Editor San Francisco Call: In a recent
speech Mr. Phelan says:
I do not think that the courts are above
criticism, for we have an elected Judiciary.
Are they to sit in their places of power and
say or do what they please without any voice
from the people?
The people are responsible for the character
of the Judges, and when they desert the cause
of the people we can bide our time and beat
them when â ey come up for re-election.
There are two of them running for o.nce
now. Beat them and so uphold the hands of
good government, xhere Is a way of inter
preting the law In favor of the people, and
there Is a way of Interpreting it against the
people. When a court arrogates to itself the
power to say what the lawmakers Intended to
enact, then the court is arrogating to Itself
legislative power and Is giving us what Is
known as judge-made law.
Although not clearly expressed, the
meaning is unmistakable. The court Is
to take any view of the law which the
people at the time wish them to. They
are to listen to and heed popular clamor.
That Mr. Phelan's audience so under
stood him there is no doubt.
liis complaint is based upon the re
cent decision in the case of Fitch vs. the
Board of Supervisors, as a result of which
the present Board of Supervisors remain
in office. Mr. Phelan and others had ob
tained a judgment ousting them. The
movement was inaugurated by Mr. Phe
lan, another gentleman who acted as
plaintiff In the suit brought for that purv
pose, and some few othersâ not more than
a dozen in all. That is not enough to
warrant any of them in writing himself
"We, the people," and reviling the Judges
as deserters of the people when the court
renders an opinion adverse to their views.
The plaintiff in that action Is a temper
ate, law-abiding citizen. He thought he
had law for tne judgment he obtained.
But as yet he has not been heard to say
he wished the court to depart from well
settled rules and decide according to
clamor from the outside. So that it does
not appear but what "We, the people,"
means Mr. Phelan himself.
Therefore, even assuming that the court
should heed popular clamor, It does not
yet appear that the people have spoken.
But I wish to take issue at the very
outset with the speaker upon the propo- |
sitlon that the court should heed popular
clamor from any source whatever. The
Justices are put there to say what the j
law Is; not to Interpret it with favor to '
any side. If the people have a right to ;
be heard In any other manner through j
their chosen representative, the Attorney
General, they have a right tp speak per- !
sonaily, for themselves, and no single one I
can be excluded from the court. They I
have a right to parade through the halls
of justice, as did the Paris mob through
the chamber of the National Assembly,
and overawe the court by force of num
bers and threats to do what they wish in
the excitement of the momentâ to do
what the cooler judgment of the people
might later regret. .Nobody with any self
respect can permit this. Every Judge
whoso name is spoken with honor has
refused to be influenced by popular
clamor, while every one who has hark
ened to it is mentioned with contempt.
Mr. Phelan is in error when he says
the court has no right to arrogate to it
self the power to say what the lawmakers
inu-nded to enact. That is just what they
aro there for. And that intention, aa it
has many times been said, is to be as
certained from the words of the law
itself; by reference to the law repealed,
to see what it was intended to abolish
and the decisions in former cases. These
make a rule of decision which the court
cannot choose but follow. It has no
right to depart from it even for the ac
complishment of a temporary good, and
even though a temporary evil may re
sult.
The people of this State have made a
constitution which provides that we shall
have a Legislature to MAKE the laws;
courts to interpret them, and say what
they mean (not always easy); and an ex- j
ecutive to carry them out. When the
court departs from the terms of the law
to effect a temporary good, it nullifies it
to that extent; and since succeeding 1
courts must follow the construction thus
given the court in effect amends the law.
This is to trench upon the province of the
Legislature and to make, rather than in
terpret, the law. It is impertinent and
unlawful.
The court has only to say what the law
Is. If it is wrong, the people will take
care of that in the house of their elected
delegates â the Legislature. The court is
not called upon Dy the constitution to
make law for them; but only to tell them
what it is, in order that they may amend
it if it does not suit them.
Even a bad law is binding until repeal
ed. It is binding upon the court and
upon every citizen. It is the law-abiding
spirit which upholds officers and courts
in enforcing a statute until changed in
an orderly and legal manner which
makes self-government a success among
Anglo-Saxon peoples, and so difficult
among the Latins. Such people allow no
law, constitutional or other, to interfere j
with the pursuit of the present object. I
He is no friend who urges us to follow
such examples. Without this law-abiding
spirit, the most solemn constitutions ana
laws make but a rope of sand; and any
thing which weakens the sentiment, even
for the greatest possible temporary good.
Is a distinct injury to the foundation or
our institutions.
It Is a fact which Mr. Phelan does not
montion that not one word of either the
main or the concurring opinion in the de
cision referred to was written by either
of the Justices now arraigned. It waa
concurred in by the entire court â seven
Judges. They merely said, "We concur."
In viow of this his condemnation extends
to the whole court, and to every single
member of it. Ts it likely that all of them
deserted the people? Is it not more like
ly that one manâ Mr. Phelan â is misled
by passion, or disappointment, or error
of Judgment?
There is one gentleman in the court who
Is a Democrat; and whose honesty, good
judgment and devotion to his country have
never been questioned. Justice Temple,
also concurred in the opinion; he said, "I
concur." Yet for holding the same opin
ion as this eminent Jurist, Justices van
Fleet and McFarland are reviled as be
trayers of the people, who should be beat
en at the polls.
In conclusion, a word or two as to the
much criticized decision.
The constitution provides that the Board
of Supervisors shall fix water rates in
February of each year; and that they
shall take effect upon the first of July
next thereafter. The Legislature added
as a penalty, that if the board did not
fix the rates it should be convicted of
malfeasance In office and ousted. That
suit for that purpose could be brought by
any interested party. The board did not
fix the rates in February, but did before
the Ist of July, and when the time came
there were new rates to go into effect
at the time provided by law. On July
15, which was after the duty enjoined by
law had been performed, Mr, Fitch's suit
was brought. The court said the terra
"interested party" means some one who
is harmed by the failure of the board to
act. That when the suit was brought the
rates had been fixed, and were in force,
and that the plaintiff was, therefore, not
damaged, and so not an Interested party
within the meaning of the law. Before
July 1 the old rates were in force. Upon
the Ist of July the new ones were ready;
so that whoever else might have a right
to complain, a ratepayer could not, tor
the simple reason that all he could ask
was that he know at the proper time
how much coifld be lawfully taken from
him for the water used. Mr. Fitch sued

Briefly given, the above is the gist or
a long opinion. A controversy between
private persona would have beÂ«n decided
upon just those principles. It is hardly
necessary to*say that the same rule musj.
apply in all cases.
But there was another fatal objection
to the judgment which was pointed out
by Justice Garoutte, and which would
have caused its reversal if the first point
had not. The board had been convicted
of malfeasance in office as an entiretyâ all
of its individual members. Conviction of
malfeasance in office disfranchises nic
officer forever afterward. Three members
had sought earnestly to do their duty.
Yet these innocent three were disgraced
in law and disfranchised along with the
guilty. Such a law is contrary to the code
of every civilized nation. It is unconsti
tutional, unjust, outrageous, and no court
with any pretensions to fairness of mind
would sustain it. ..-_.Â»
Yet it is for failure to uphold this judg
ment and such a law that the Mayor says
of two of the unanimous seven: "There
are two of them running for office now.
Beat them, and so uphold the hands of
good government." Good government, we
are to infer, consists in punishing all
alike, the innocent with the guilty, and
refusing to apply a rule of decision to a
case in which the speaker felt a personal
interest, which the court would not apply
in any other case. . ..
I commend the moral courage of the
court that rendered this decision The de
cision itself I commend to the -voters of
the State as the best possible reason for
the re-election of the gentlemen against
whom the above remarks were directed.
Witâ¢such Judges, the fundamental rules
nf ritrht and wrong at least are safe, but
"/those whos^ "lection Mr. Phelan seeks
would uphold such a law we had better
leave them In retirement SA . RGENT .

THE GOSPEL OF
REPUBLICANS
Big Speakers Deal
With Party Truths.
LABORING MEN LISTENED
PHELAN -DONE BROWN 1 ' BY A
CONVERTED DEMOCRAT.
Attendance at Republican District
Meeting's Waxes Larger and
the Speakers Are Accorded
Hearty Reception.
There was another rousing Republi- j
can meeting in the Potrero last even- j
ing; another meeting of the laboring ;
men. Fully half a thousand of them
gathered last evening at the Potrero j
Opera-house, and many of them
brought their wives, to listen to Charles
L. Patton and others on the issues of
the day.
The speakers of the evening were
Julius Kahn, Alfred Morgenstern,
Charles L. Patton and John Lenehan.
Mr. Morgenstern began his speech
â with an argument for a national vote
of confidence in President McKinley
and in the course that the executive
has pursued through the late war with
Spain. He called attention to the fact
that it is only by electing Republican
Congressmen and a Republican Legis
lature to choose California's Senator
that this can be done.
Leaving State for local issues Mr.
Morgenstern said: "James D. Phelan,
my friends, thought that the Supreme'
Court was an angelic body until it com
pelled him to pay $63,000 in taxes that
he was trying 10 evade. Since then he
has had little use for that body, and he
has been holding it up to ridicule
through all of this campaign. Ho is
also holding up the railroad as a fac
tor in the coming election in spite of the
fact that he saved that company thou
sands of dollars by tearing up its old
track on Market street at the city's ex
pense at just the time when it was
about to tear it up on its own account.
And you don't want to forget that after
he had it torn up he paved a strip eight
feet wide with bitumen at the expense
of the city that the company by its
franchise was obliged to pave.
"The very convention that nominated
Mr. Phelan was contrary to the law;
it was the product of the Committee I
of One Hundred, an aggregation of job- j
chasers that had no excuse for its ex- j
Jstenee but Phelan's appointment. He i
is trying to square himself on that j
proposition by explaining that there is
no primary law under which he and
the Democrats could have proceeded.
Mr. Phelan is a lawyer and he knows
as well as any one knows that the Por
ter primary law is in full force and ef
iect, and that it affords voters and
parties full protection if they care to
â¢ it. It also provides severe pen
alties for those who transgress it.
"Another point that is counting
largely against Mr. Phelan is that he
has not the support of any one of the
reputable papers of this city. His sup
port is the Examiner and the Bulletin. 1
For me to stand here and argue to you
that the Examiner is not a reputable
paper wc-uld be an insult to your good
sense. As it is a well-known fact that
the Bulletin is out for whatever price
it can get there is also no need for me !
to give any attention to that quarter, j
These are the only papers in this city
that are lendins to Phelan any sup- j
port in his fight, and their support is
such that it would be shunned by any
Republican to whom it was offered."
Julius Kahn was next to speak. In a !
masterly manner he laid before his au
dlence the things that Mr. McKinley \
has accomplished in the last year; the !
height that the President has attained i
in the list of the world's statesmen. He
spoke of the necessity of retaining the
lands that this country has acquired
by the force of its arms and the justice
of its course in the late war. He
pointed out the many things that are
due this coast at the hands of the Gov
ernment and concluded with the argu
ment that only by electing a Congress
and a Senate in accord and sympathy
with the administration could they be
obtained and made the most of.
Following the speech of Mr. Kahn,
the Waterman sisters sang a number of
popular campaign songs. The applause
that rewarded them had scarcely quiet
ed when John Lenehan took the stand.
Mr. Lenehan was a Democratic repre
sentative in the Stale Senate in 1887,
and a stanch Democrat he has always
been until California Democracy lost
its identity. He stayed with it even
then, but when the party was turned
over to Maguire he gave up the at
tempt, and over since has stood for his
own belief and convictions. His con
victions are Republican, and he made
a rousing Republican speech last even-
I- er.
Ho opened with the broad statement
that if James D. Phelan was elected it
would be by the Republican vote. The
Democrats, he said, were solid against
him. With a knowledge of the man of
whom he spoke he held Mr. Phelan up
to the lim*> light of public opinion and
showed him, as he is, in his numerous
characters, to his hearers.
Mr. Patton got in late and his speech
was short. It consisted merely of a
pledge to subserve the best interests of
the peonie if he was elected. He was
received with the heartiest of greetings,
and lost nothing by his brevity.
William A. Deane. Judge Joachimsen,
T. H. Graham and Judge Low also
sroke, as did I. J. Trumaji and Asa
W>lls. The minor candidates were all
on hand, and icy wen all received
w the greatest enthusiasm.

cratic Meetings.

are among the speakers. To-morrow
night will be the big meeting under the

leges and by General W. H. L. Barnes.
The Democrats have arranged for two

lan. Franklin F. Lane. Barclay Henley

"JIMMY THE HEFTER " ASPIRES TO BE AN OCTOPUS.
MAGUIRE AN
APOSTLE OF
HENRY GEORGE
Kowalsky Condemns
Single Tax.
EVILS OF ITS ADOPTION
ELOQUENT ADDRESS DELIVERED
AT ODD FELLOWS' HALL.
James H. Barry Denounced as a
Henchman of Maguire's and an.
Advocate of His Doc
trines.
Odd Fellows' Hall was crowded to its
utmost capacity last evening by enthusi
astic Republicans from all parts of the
city, who assembled to hear the an
nounced address of Colonel Henry I.
Kowalsky on the issues of <the present
campaign.
After being introduced to the audience,
the attofney said: "The nomination of
James G. Maguire, the leading apostle of
the doctrine of Henry George, candidate
for Governor of California, has introduced
tlie Question of the destruction of private
property in land as an issue. The Demo
cratic party in Indorsing the man adopts I
this principle, because for many years I
Maguire has asserted and still asserts !
that it is the only remedy for industrial j
evils. Moreover, the Democratic party
openly commends the course of Maguire
in Congress, which has been spent almost
entirely in speeches and efforts to substi
tute the single tax on land In place of all
other taxes or tariffs. In addition to this,
Mr. Maguire, in a letter over his own
signature printed in the San Francisco I
Examiner May 6, ISS6, headed, 'The Next
Great Issue,' declares the single tax is
near at. hand, and in such letter says:
" 'Already the single taxers demand
that practical effort lie commenced imme
diately to force the issue into politics in
California.'
"Judge Magulre, in a speech before the
Single Tax Society in San Francisco on
September 6, 1891, published in the San
Francisco Call of September 7, 1891, esti
mates that under his system of single
tax, estimating the Federal Government
expenses as low as those of ISB6, viz.,
$242,483,138, the local tax rate would be at
least 4 per cent on all land upon the
assessed value, and according to his own
figures there made if based upon the
present expenditures of the Federal Gov
ernment the tax upon land under the sys
tem favored by him would be 5 per cent
or more, but he has repeatedly declared
that the whole rental value shall be taken
for taxes.
"Now, let me tell you. if Maguire was
as ambitious in a religious way as he is
politically ne would adopt about as many
religions as he has parties if he thought
he could get to heaven. I can see him
expecting a blessing -from Rev. Dr. Voor
sanger on a Saturday, and on a Sunday I
can see him beseeching Father Gallagher,
and when the moon is full I can see him
in a Chinese josshouse before the great
joss, licking the varnish off a fatted
pig so as to give evidence to the heathen
that he Is In full accord with them and
their ideas. I can see him gorging him
self with hog enough to keep an ordinary
Arctic explorer warm for six months.
Next. 1 can see him with his book
written on the Catholic church,
for which he ought to have two
covers, one of which to present
to the Catholic Irishman wno believes in
the Churoh of Rome, with a nleasing
preface, in order that he may hold him
as a friend, and the other cover merely
the reverse, which he can show to the
Irishman who doesn't believe in the
Church of Rome, but who is ready for a
fight on the 12th day of july. I say
again, if Mr. Maguire were as religiously
ambitious as he Is politically his amount
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, OCTOBER 28. 1898.
of hypocrisy is great enough to land him
clear into the middle of heaven, or else,
in the rebound, into the regions below."
BROKE UP REPUBLICAN RALLY.
County Candidates Routed at Ala-
zneda by Organized Disturbers.
ALAMEDA, Oct. 27.â An organized at
tempt was made last nipht to break up
the Republican meeting at West End. ]t
is almost impossible to locate the perpe
trators of the offense, but they succeeded
in their efforts and stopped the speech-
making before half of the programme had
been carried out. Clarence Patton, a well
known character about the eastern end of
town, was used by the conspirators to
carry out their plans. He was togged out
regardless of style, and when the meeting
opened was seated on the stage with the
candidate?. He was a stranger to a West
End audience, and when during each in
termission there were cries of "Patton!
Pattort!" there were inquiries as to who
the individual was. It was whispered
about that he was an independent candi
date for City Marshal, who was backed
up by the younger element. As the calls
for Patton became more insistent the
chairman, all unconscious of the purpose,
introduced the young man to the audi
ence. Patton played his part well and
started to tell the crowd that he was a
candidate for Marshal, when his voice
waÃ lost in the cheers that came from
those who had put up the job. The noise
lasted for over ten minutes. The county
candidates left the hall In disgust, and
when the noise subsided Patton and his
friends were alone in the building.
REPUBLICANS AT OCEAN VIEW.
A Rousing Reception Tendered to
the State and City Nominees.
A most enthusiastic reception was ten
dered to the Republican nominees, both
State and municipal, by the residents of
Ocean View at Murphy's Hall last night.
A brass band and a huge brush bonfire
indicated the place of meeting as the
San Francisco and San Mateo electric
cars unloaded their human freight at the
doors of the hall.
H. E. Griffith, president of the Ocean
View Republican Club, had the honor of
presiding at the meeting. In addition to
the presence of the popular nominee for
Mayor, Charles L. Patton. and Henry S.
Martin, the next Sheriff, there were also
C S Laumeister, nominee for Railroad
Commissioner. C. F. Curry, nominee for
Secretary of State, was warmly greeted.
Mr. Pattnn reviewed the platform of
the Republican party and its nominees.
I J Truman, nominee for Treasurer, as
sured the people that they would
be fairly dealt with by the party. D. J.
Murphy, nominee for District Attorney,
followed. Thomas F. Graham, one of the
nominees for Police Judge met with a
very flattering reception and made a good
impression by his short and pointed
speech. Other speakers were A. B Tread
well, Judge Frank Kerrigan . H. N. Cle
ment. Judge C. A. Low Martin F Frag
ley G W. Collins and Henry S. Martin.
There Is But One E. L. Fitzgerald.
The votars of San Francisco who have
followed the exposures of The Call re
garding the record of Labor Commis
sioner E. L. Fitzgerald, the Democratic
candidate for Public Administrator,
should be careful not to confound the
name with that of Attorney General Wil
liam F. Fitzgerald, the Republican can
didate for the office of City and County
Attorney. The men are not relation:*
Attorney General Fitzgerald is an hon
orable gentleman, whose record as a pub
lic servant entitles him to the respect
and admiration of all good citizens.
A Mistake in Names.
In a report of the proceedings before
the Democratic Central Club published
In these columns yesterday Charles Eldel
man, one of the speakers, was reported
as making a humorous reference to a visit
be and "Tarpey" had made through the
district in search of votes. Instead of
"Tarpey" the name should have read
"Treacey," and in justice to all parties
concerned this correction is now made.

thier, John J. Conlon, Edward Aigeltlnger

GOOD REfIDiNG FOR VALENTINE.
AT a recent meeting of the National Association
of Freight Commissioners, held in Detroit,
much consideration was given to the attitude
of express companies toward the war tax. Criticism
upon such as refused, after a Wells-Fargo style, to
pay their share of the tax was general and severe.
The result of the deliberations was embodied in a
pamphlet which would make excellent reading for
President Valentine. If he has no copy he is wel
come to the loan oi a copy now in possession of The
Call.
Among reports read at the meeting were some
showing that certain express companies, notably
those of the South, had cheerfully complied with
legal enactments, had not striven to impose upon
their patrons, nor, attempting to misconstrue the law,
supported their contentions with Scripture quota
tions. Companies operating in Texas complied,
whether cheerfully or not matters little, since re
fusal to do so would have subjected the officials to
heavy fine. Here is an excerpt from a circular sent
out by the Railroad Commissioner of that State as a
warning:
"That any express company that requires the
shipper to pay for this stamp, in addition to the regu
lar tariff rates in force, is to that extent guilty of ex
tortion and liable to be prosecuted, and a penalty of
$500 for each offense collected of them."
Here is another, which was regarded as applicable:
"Every express company doing business in this
State which shall demand or receive a greater com
pensation than that which may be prescribed and
fixed by the said Railroad Commission for the trans
portation of any class or kind of property * * *
shall be deemed guilty of extortion and shall forfeit
and pay to the State of Texas a sum not to exceed
$500 for each offense."
Now California sustains a Board of Railroad Com
missioners. The Wells-Fargo Company is a common
carrier, and as such as directly within the jurisdic
tion of this body as the Texas companies are within
the jurisdiction of the one whose views and powers
have been cited. The Legislature also has a right to
participate in the regulation of a common carrier and
to force it to conduct itself honestly and decently, to
the elimination of comic Valentine methods.
The gentlemen at Detroit reached the decision that
the best method of protection against corporate greed
was the inauguration of a parcels post, but this is an
other subject.
PARETICS IN SPASMS.
SEVERAL paretics whose degeneracy has taken
the form of a desire to write are making a
feeble and babbling ado over an editorial para
graph in this paper. The paragraph in question con
gratulated Mrs. Botkin that she was not going to
Delaware to be tried, adding that a murder trial in
California was apt not to be a serious affair.
Thereupon arose a paretic, grasping a pen in his
red right hand, and said the paragraph had been writ
ten out of sympathy for the woman. Anybody with
intelligence acute enough to take a hint from the
tumbling of a house knew at a glance that the para
graph was a tribute to the laxity of justice when it
gets hold of a murderer. For the Botkin woman
there is not the least occasion for sympathy. If
guilty, hanging is too good for her.
But another scribe, impelled to rashness by the
softening of his brain or the absence of this useful
organ, declared that the attack upon the courts was
vile. He made the declaration in italics, and seemed to
feel real badly. Oh, well, possibly our courts are not
perfect. When three years are consumed in hang
ing a Durrant, when the slayer of a, police officer is
permitted to go free on evidence nobody doubted to
be perjury; when Mrs. Shattuck lures a man to her
house for the purpose of killing him, and does so
without excuse, afterward escaping punishment; when
a soldier kills his tent mate and is acquitted, there is
reason to think that a trial for murder is more serious
to the taxpayer than to anybody else. One woman in
recent years has been punished in San Francisco for
murder. She is now in San Quentin. She was a
silly creature, weak in body as in mind, moneyless,
friendless, not a dashing beauty. She had no in
fluence.
When there is such difficulty in convicting a mur
derer on the plainest evidence, while lawyers will
quibble so long as there is a fee in sight, it is surely
reasonable to suppose that in a case hedged about
with so many complications as the present one. the
trial will be long and, in all likelihood, ineffective.
Probably Mrs. Botkin would have found conditions
different in Delaware, She is to be congratulated;
the oublie isn't.
MAGUIRE ON PERSONALITIES.
JUDGE MAGUIRE has fallen to complaining
that his opponents have dealt in personalities.
He is> the last man who should make that com
plaint. As a rule a public man who himself avoids
j personalities toward others is exempt from them him
! self. But no public man in California has dealt in
more offensive personalities toward his opponents
than Judge Maguire. When he bolted the Demo
cratic ticket in 1886 he was rancorous and not decent
in his personal treatment. He called that gentlest of
gentlemen, Frank McCoppin, "a piece of carrion,"
when McCoppin's only offense was that he had re
ceived the regular Democratic nomination for Con
gress. In the same campaign he personally abused
Mr. Pond, against whom he was running Hinton, as
a bolter, for Mayor.
AY hen he sat down in his study to write his book
on "Ireland and the Pope" his abusive disposition
got the better of his discretion. Professing to write
truthfully on 800 years of history, stretching from
Pope Urban IV to Leo XIII, he displayed none of
the calm and judicial spirit which becomes the his
torian, but dealt in invective from start to finish. He
called the Pope "the serpent of the Vatican, sinking
his fangs in the heart of liberty." He called the car
dinals "red-capped hounds," and exhausted the vocab
ulary of abuse.
He betrays the same tendency in his discussion of
all public matters. In his copious speeches and
writings on the confiscation of land he continually
and with a wearisome and vituperative reiteration
calls the land-owner "a monopolist," a "cunning rob
ber," the "despoiler of God's children," an enemy of
"God's law," the "foe of society," who is to be pun
ished for investing in land by being "expelled" from
what he pretends to own. Vituperation and abuse,
personal detraction, the ascription to others of un
worthy motives, exude from him as naturally as red
sweat from a hippopotamus. In the present cam
paign he began with gross personal abuse of every
delegate to the Republican State Convention by call
ing them the tools and chattels of the Southern Pa
cific Railroad. He began personally decrying his op
ponent, Mr. Gage, by accusing him of bargaining
with the railroad for his nomination and intending,
if elected, to be the minion of that corporation in
stead of the servant of the people. No more gross,
abusive and insulting charge could be made against
a gentleman. But Maguire made it and has repeated
it. directly or by cowardly, unbecoming and unmanly
innuendo, in nearly every speech he has made.
Now what of the personal abuse of himself, of
which this venomously vituperative candidate com
plains? It consists solely of the publication of his
speeches and writings and holding him responsible
for what he has said himself, and for a fair and just
interpretation of it.
When he said that anarchists and nihilists were
worthy people and desirable immigrants whom he
hoped never to see excluded "from this land of the
free heart's hope and home," that utterance could
have but one meaning. It was an indorsement of the
anarchist and nihilist method and propaganda, and
the offer of an asylum in this country to the foes of
all government and the assassins of all who repre
sent law and order.
When he said that the land-owner had no right to
the land he had bought and "beautified and made fer
tile by his labor, but could be at any moment ex
pelled from it by its lawful owner, society," he de
clared for the communal confiscation of land without
compensation to the owner. If being "expelled" by
the commune is not confiscation, what is it? His
declaration was in form identical with Nicholas Ba
kounin's definition of anarchy â "Everything is every
body's."
It is no abuse of a man to hold him responsible
for what he says. If a man when a candidate for
office dodges responsibility for every public declara
tion he made before his nomination, but one conclu
sion can be formed concerning him â that he is a
mouthing fellow, so insincere that he will stand by
nothing that he says. If Judge Maguire would now
say from the stump: "Yes, I said the Pope is a ser
pent with poison fangs, and I stand by it. I said the
land-owner is a monopolist and a robber of God's
children, who can be justly expelled from his land
without compensation, though it may be a hardship
to him, and I stand by it," everybody would respect
his adherence to those expressions of his belief,
though the belief would be odious to a majority of
men.
If Maguire choose to treat his record as a dog does
the tin kettle tied to his tail and try to run away from
it, it is no personal abuse to call attention to the
kettle. â 
fl ONE-SIDED COMPOIGN.
ONE of the most striking and significant fea
tures of this campaign is the contrast in the
strength presented by the opposing parties on
the stump. It is a condition never before seen in
California in this generation. On the Republican
side is a host of orators of the highest eminence in
politics and in professional life, and on the other is
one stump speaker of note â Maguire howling in the
wilderness alone and forsaken.
The weakness of the Democratic campaign in this
respect is remarkable, for however that party may
lack for statesmen, it has never before lacked for
orators of considerable ability. At the present time
it has in its ranks many speakers who if they took
the stump would attract large audiences, but they are
silent in this fight. They refuse to be eloquent or
even to be loquacious for fusion, single tax, anarchy
and Maguireism.
The Democratic masses in this campaign look in
vain for the old leaders, listen in vain for the voices
of the old orators. English, Foote, Geary, Irish,
Caminetti, Reddy, Clunie and many another of local
fame and note â where are they?
There is surely some significance in this silence on
the part of the noted leaders of California Democracy.
Men who have been identified with a party through
out their political career â politicians whose fortunes
are bound up with the success of the party â do not
forsake it for nothing. Maguireism has struck old
Democracy a hard blowâ it has knocked it speechless.
Los Angeles is said to have a healer who poses as
a representative of divine power, and under whose
gentle ministration people are taught to escape
human ills by dying of starvation. Los Angeles also
having a jail, a question naturally arises as to why
the healer should be outside of it.
Kitchener and Marchand seem to be about the most
peaceable representatives of England and France, and
yet only a few days ago they were credited with a
desire to eat each other up. Possibly the yellow
journalist is not wholly an American product.
A Chicago man being late to dinner became in
volved in a quarrel with the lady of the house, dur
ing which he killed both her and himself. By this
time the dinner was so cold that it wa3 spoiled, such
waste lending a moral to the episode.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, OCTOBER 28, 1898.
ONE of the most barefaced tricks of the cam
paign is that which has been perpetrated by
the circulation of a so-calied "regular teach
ers' ticket" of candidates for School Directors. While
the trick has been carried out with some slyness in
an underhand way, it is not difficult to decide as to
its authorship. It carries on its face unmistakable
marks of its origin.
The ticket puts up something of a pretense to be
non-partisan in its nature, for it includes Republicans
as well as Democrats among its candidates. The pre
tense, however, is but a thin one, for there are but
four Republicans to eight Democrats. Such a
ticket, it will be seen, would give the Democrats com
plete control of the Board of Education by a two
thirds vote. It is clearly one of those tricks which,
if successful, would prove to be a snap for the work
ers of it.
That there may be no mistake in the matter we
give the names on the ticket. They appear without
any statement of the political affiliations of the can
didates, but the letters giving the initials of the
party to which each candidate belongs are added in
order that the public may understand the nature of
the scheme. The list is as follows:
William A. Kemp (R.), D. F. Keefe (D.), T. J.
Lyons (D.), J. M. Thomas (R.), Benjamin Armer
(D.), George R. Armstrong (R.), H. W. Branden
stein (D.), A. A. Cavagnaro (D.), J. J. Conlon (R.),
Dr. P. J. Conran (D.), William M. Cannon (D.), and
Charles P. Eells (D.).
A copy of the ticket has been sent to nearly every
public school teacher in the city with the request
that it be supported in the coming election. Several
of the tickets sent out were accompanied by remarks
giving the impression that they emanated from the
office of the clerk of the Board of Education, but this
it appears was a part of the trick.
The fraud, though cunning in design, was too
clumsy in execution to succeed. The teachers who
received the ticket were surprised and began at once
to make inquiries at the office of the board for an
explanation. It was then found that the thing was a
trick. The teachers not only have had nothing to do
with it, but they distinctly repudiate it. The whole
scheme was clearly an attempt to make use of the
teachers to elect a Democratic majority of the board.
We will leave the eight Democrats who would have
profited by the success of the trick to explain the
thing to their colleagues on the Democratic ticket
who were left out of the circular, and before the ex
planations are over it will probably be found by the
authors that the trick is a roorback.
The only way to provide for a good Board of Edu
cation and to assure the right administration of our
schools is to vote for the straight Republican ticket.
Every candidate for the board was selected by the
Republican convention solely because of his personal
fitness for the place They resort to no tricks nor
frauds in making the canvass. They stand for hon
esty in the campaign and in office.
SECOND CONGRESSIONAL DISTRICT.
Iâ\EPUBLICANS1 â \EPUBLICANS of the Second Congressional
lâv' District have an opportunity of' rectifying the
* * mistake of two years ago and sending to the
House of Representatives a stanch upholder of the
administration in place of the Democrat whom they
elected at that time. It is an opportunity which no
loyal member of the party can afford to neglect. The
issue is far-reaching. It appeals to patriotism as well
as to local interests and popular welfare.
Grave problems of foreign as well as of domestic
politics will confront the administration during the
term of the Congress that is to be elected this fall. It
is for the voters to determine whether the President,
when he meets those issues, shah have the support
of a House of Representatives friendly to his policies
and willing to support him in loyalty, or a House
dominated by a majority antagonistic to the Presi
dent and more desirous of hindering than of aiding
him to make his administration a success and a benefit
to the nation.
All the questions now before the people in the
arena of national politics are summed up in the one
great issue of supporting the administration. Every
candidate for Congress is either for the President or
against him. It is for the voters to determine whether
they will be represented at Washington for the next
two years by a friend of the administration or an
opponent.
Foreign nations are waiting to see what Americans
will do in this crisis. The elections are to show
whether our people are changeable and fickle, or
whether they will stand by their Government until the
questions growing out of the war are settled and the
old issues of tariff and finance closed for this genera
tion.
To lead them in the contest the Republicans of the
Second District have a young and vigorous cham
pion. Frank D. Ryan is one of the most noted of
the Native Sons of the State. He has long been
prominent in public life, and holds a high rank at
the bar. As District Attorney of Sacramento he has
made a record which attests at once his ability as a
lawyer and his fidelity to the public service, nor can
it be doubted that in the wider field of Congressional
activity he will show an equal efficiency, integrity and
zeal.
With such a candidate to represent the administra
tion and with such issues at stake, there ought to be
no question as to what the voters of the Second Dis
trict will do on election day. The Democrats are
making a still hunt and fighting hard for every seat
in sight. Republicans cannot afford to be indifferent
or over-confident at any point of the contest.
"Stand by the President" should be the motto of the
canvass from this time on.
People of Connecticut are angry because no battle
ship has been named after their State. Yet here is Cal
ifornia, where the best battleships the world knows
are turned out as easily as Connecticut ever made
nutmegs, not getting its name attached to so much as
a converted tug, and not raising a row about it,
either.
By the opportunity afforded through murders com
mitted by Patrick Keenan and Patrick O'Shea the
courts will be able to demonstrate the speed with
which two cold-blooded assassins, concerning whose
guilt there is no shadow of doubt, can be dropped
about six feet.
People who witnessed the drowning of a team
ster at the Jackson-street wharf seemed to be stirred
by the spectacle, but there never came to them the
conviction that to rescue a man from drowning is
considered not only proper but laudable.
If Spain really contemplates a renewal of hostili
ties let it reflect that the victories won by the making
of spirited remarks and tearing of the hair have been
comparatively few.
The only surprising feature of the report that
President Faure had been murdered was that it was
not true.
A ROORBACK TICKET.
THE WOODEN HORSE
To the Editor of The Call: The power of the people may be used
against the people. This is a proposition that plain people, must always
keep in mind. This government was made for the plain people, for you and
for me, and not for the politicians. The men who made the government
took all pains to prevent the politician getting so much power that he
might be dangerous to us. For that reason they divided political power
among many officials; they took care that every few years those officials
should return their power to the people and they cultivated the growth of
gieat political parties so that the people might have the power of choice.
The boss tries to get around these devices first by controlling as many
of the officials ac possible, secondly by nominating for office those that
will serve Mm, and thirdly by seizing the party machinery so that no other
leader may have an opportunity to come to the surface. This is Mr. Phe
lan's method. Other bosses have tried it, but none have brought it to such
perfection as he. Some have controlled conventions, some have mastered
Supervisors, some have been able to reach the judiciary, some have owned
the Sheriff or the County Clerk, but never in this city has there been a
man strong enough to own convention, Supervisors, School Board, Sheriff,
County Clerk, Assessor, Auditor, the judiciary, in a word everything ex
cept the Morgue. This is now Mr. Phelan's aim. To reach this end he ap
peals to the plain peopleâ to you and to me. He asks for your vote and
my vote to attain his ambition.
IN THE AMERICAN SYSTEM
There is only one legitimate way for obtaining elective office, and that is
by obtaining the most votes. But Mr. Phelan is not satisfied with that way.
In the first place, it is too uncertain and Mr. Phelan is not taking chances.
In the second place it does not make Mr. Phelan the sole source of power.
Of course he cannot entirely dispense with our votes, but we may learn
his desire and his inclinations where he thinks he can do without us.
Honesty may be put to many dishonest purposes. There was never a vir
tue yet that did not have its counterfeit presentment. Political honesty
is such a taking cry that it is no wonder that pretenders have made the
most of it. For a long time the plain people â you and I â have had our eye
on the professional honest politicians. One of the very peculiar facts con
nected with our local politics is that there have always been three honest
men- in our Boards of Supervisors. This is as constant as a natural law.
It has held so long that the memory of man runneth not to the contrary.
Mathematics and honesty have no connection as far as is known to our
philosophy. Why, therefore, there should be three and only three cannot
be explained by any inherent property of this virtue of honesty in gen
eral, or of political honesty in particular. Of course we know that if out
of twelve men only nine can fit in a room the other three must remain out
side the door. The three outsiders may explain their position by whatever
virtue they please â honesty, temperance, fortitude, magnanimity or fear of
the Lordâ any old virtue will do. But the plain citizen will have his own
explanation and his explanation will be based on the physical fact that a
room that can hold only nine does not need more than nine to make it full.
HENCE THE THREE HONEST MEN
Who appear with painful monotony In every Board of Supervisors are re
garded with suspicious interest by the plain citizenâ by you and by me.
We listen to their thunderous indignation as they denounce the solid nine
that have broken into some treasury or another. We take in their pas
sionate protestations that they have never cast a sidelong eye on a treas
ury â their thoughts are as virgin of treasuries as the colored person's of
fiction are of hen roosts. We smile when we hear them describe the in
famy of combines, for we are not so old that we have forgotten our child
hood's fable of the fox who gave his opinion of sour grapes.
In the present Board of Supervisors there were three painfully honest
menâ Mr. Dodge, Mr. Clinton and Mr. Lackmann. Their honesty has for
now two years reverberated between the Twin Peaks and Goat Island.
There is no one in this city ignorant of their virtues, for have they not
sat in a row in the public places like Justice with a patch on their eyes
and unlike Justice with a placard on their bosoms bearing the legend, "I
am blind, but honest"?
The chief characteristic of these three Supervisors during the past two
years, besides their honesty, has been
THEIR DEVOTION TO MR. PHELAN.
They were men who would support his policy. Two were Democrats and
one was Republican but they were all of the Phelan party. Whatever he
wanted they did, and without him they did nothing.
In the new Jerusalem which Mr. Phelan intends to let down upon this
peninsula from the heaven of his own honesty three such faithful servi
tors should have a high place. One might sit at his right hand and the
other on his left and the third would be convenient as the footstool of his
feet. Such services as theirs deserved a reward, and a reward that was
sure.
Of course the people are in theory the last judges of honesty. They
often in elections are rendered distrustful of professions that grow too
loud. It is possible that they might not take the three honest men at
their words. They might think it quite possible that the next time the
room that only holds nine was empty these three men might by mistake
wander inâ for are they not blind? But Mr. Phelan does not believe in
trusting the people any more than he can help. He knew a way to re
ward his servants with a sure reward.
When a member of one party Is dissatisfied with the party's candidates
he always has the recourse of voting the ticket of the other party. This
is one of the advantages of the existence of parties.
NO PARTY CAN AFFORD TO IGNORE THE PLAIN CITIZEN
Entirelyâ you and I can take our revenge by supporting the least obstrep
erous organization. But this recourse is taken away from us altogether
if both parties nominate the same Gandidates. We can do nothing then
but submit.
This was Mr. Phelan's brilliant scheme for rewarding those who had
served him well. He would put them on both tickets. No matter hov>
they had conducted themselves on other matters, the people could not re
buke them. They had been his faithful servants and this virtue covered
all other sins. They might have neglected their constituents, betrayed
their principles, flown in the face of justice, but the people could not
reach them. They were
SAFE WITHIN THE AWFUL SHADOW OF THE PLUG HAT.
Against them none dare lift a hand. With the Republican, Mr. Lackmann
the scheme succeeded. He was nominated by the Republican convention
and by the committee of one hundred. The committee of one hundred
claims to be the Democratic party of San Francisco. If it has any reason
for existence it is to nominate Democrats to office. If it does not nomi
nate Democrats it should go out of business and become a non-partisan
party or something else. Plain people like you and me may sometimes
fail to see the use of bringing national party names into municipal affairs
But as long as they are brought in, because we are plain people, we wish
to see them plainly and honestly used. A Democratic convention that
nominates Republicans is a mockery, a delusion and a snare.
But you and I see clearly enough that the virtues to which the commit
tee of one hundred looked were not the virtues known as Democracy, but
subserviency to Mr. Phelan. His cry has been, "Give me men that' will
support my policy." He does not care whether they are Republicans or
Democrats as long as they do his will. When we hire servants we don't
ask their politics. Sufficient for us that they obey.
So the committee of one hundred nominated Mr. Laokmann and Mr
Lackmann has received his reward. He can sit in peace under his own vine
and fig tree and wait for November 8. No matter how many enemies he
has, the people cannot defeat him. He goes on the board not as the repre
sentative of the people but as the representative of James D. Phelan.
Mr. Phelan tried the same tactics with Mr. Clinton. Mr. Clinton is a
Democrat, and the process was not so easy as in the case of Mr. Lackmann
Mr. Phelan, who poses as the apostle of purity, whose campaign Is a con
tinued cry against the terrible evil of having bosses other than himself an"
proaches the Kelly and Crimmins outfit with the proposition to nominate
Mr. Clinton on the Republican ticket. But whether Kelly and Crimmins
could not deliver the goods, or whether the scheme was too scandalous for
them, the deal was not consummated. But. Mr. Phelan's object was the
same. No matter how many valid and serious objections might be against
Mr. Clinton, he tried to force him upon the voters of this city because of
his obedience to himself.
The plain citzen, that is you and I, may learn from this how far Mr
Phelan is prepared to go. Here we have no ordinary boss; we have a man of
talent, of wealth, of ingenuity, striving to control the whole city Why \<*
he making such efforts? To give us a good government, he say's When
the wooden horse lay outside the walls of Troy the Phelan of that'dav de
clared that if it were brought within the city the wicked Greeks could
never again molest the Trojans' peace. A plain man, like you and I sairt
"I am afraid of the Greeks even when they bring us good gifts " The nlaln
man was right. The wooden horse was filled with armed men It 'was brought
into, the town, and soon the town was but a name. Even now- nlain twT

LITERATURE
FOR MANILA
Editor The Call: I am in receipt of
a letter dated September 22 from Rev.
Francis B. Doherty, C. S. P., staff
chaplain with our troops in Manila, in
which he asks me to send whatever
papers and magazines I can spare for
the soldiers in the hospitals and bar
racks.
Will you not ask the subscribers to your
valuable paper to forward their copies
after reading to Rev. Francis B. Do
herty, C. S. P., chaplain, U. S. A., Casa
del Secretario, Molacanan, Manila,
Philippine Islands. The rates of postage
are the same as to points within the
United States.
I have taken the liberty to pass the
reverend gentleman's request to me for
literature all along the line. It would
be a disgrace if our troops at Manila
were not supplied with wholsesome
reading matter, and I trust that you
will do what you can to supply the
need. There need be no fear of a sur
plus as I think I can promise that the
overflow will go to Dewey's gallant
men. Very truly yours,
M. J. RIORDAN.
Flagstaff, Ariz., Oct. 25, 1898.

FATHER CARAHER
ANSWERS FATHER OTIS,,
DISAGBEES WITH THE REV
EREND GENTLEMAN.
Commends the Decisive Action of the
Police in Closing More Dens
of Vice Yesterday.
Father Caraher of St. Francis Church
has submitted the following reply to the
letter from Father Otis, published in yes
terday's Call:
Editor of The Callâ Dear Sir: In to-day's
issue of your journal 1 am once more as-
Bailed for the stand I have taken against
a dire evil and for my defense of thousands
of children and adults. The two letters of
my censor are remarkable only for mis
representation, passion and reckless asser
tions. In the second letter he does not, as
in the first, suggest that I change my
residence in certain circumstances, for he
knows, or should know, that I have no
rural abode, and that in no case could I
think of scampering to an Eastern nome.
This diocese is mine by adoption; here I
have labored, however feebly and unfruit
fully, for twenty-five years, and here I in
tend to end my days.
The appeal to St. Paul's -writings to sub
stantiate the fearful doctrine that the
abominations of Sodom and Gomorrah are
"kindred" crimes to buying and selling in
the temple is, to say the least, unjust to
the great apostle. St. Paul merely men
tions the sinners that shall be excluded
from heaven if they die in these specific
sins, but neither he nor any theologian
dare teach that buying and selling are
"kindred" or cognate to these abomina
tions.
Time and again my self-appointed censor
strives to make me use the word "localize,
which I have studiously avoided. I have
said that "It is my belief that filth should
be impounded and compressed instead of
diffused," and I think that any sane man
will admit the correctness of this belief.
When a building or locality is invaded by
smallpox it is quarantined, and there is
neither ingress nor egress, lest the con
tagion spread. In the case under considera
tion the evil was being rapidly diffused,
the diffusion had already reached, and
threatened to ruin, this section of the city,
and the diffusers cannot now escape from
their criminal part in the work.
My censor asks me to furnish him with
the proofs in my possession that a move
ment was on foot to dump the filth or his
locality at our doors, and offers me valuable
assistance against any such future move
ment. What effrontery, to ask me to sup
ply him with proofs! An effrontery only
equaled by his indiscretion in starting a
controversy. As to his assistance in the
future, I disdain to receive any from such
a source. Nevertheless, these proofs are in
my possession, and I am ready to present
them when the demand comes from the
proper quarter.
In writing of the destruction of Sodom
and Gomorrah I stated I was far from sug
gesting fire and sword as a remedy for the
present evil, much though I desire to see
it extinguished, but my censor leads us to
believe that he would use fire. "We cannot
say that we indorse his disapproval of the
means employed by God," etc. This language
could be easily construed to imply a threat
to burn property, if not persons, and the
man who uses it might be easily connected
with future conflagrations. Not <~,nly would
I not burn nor kill any of these unfortonate
women but I am ready to provide a home
for any one in my parish that shows signs
of repentance. I am ready to receive any
of these pitiable outcasts as our Lord re
ceived the Magdalene and as my predeces
sors in the priesthood received Mary of
Egypt and Margaret of Cortona. They are
all made to the image and likeness of God.
That image and likeness, though dis
figured, still remain, and from their
fallen state they may be raised to the glory
of heaven.
The writer most unfairly separates the word
"impounded" from the word "compressed,'
whereas I had closely connected them, and
in contradistinction to the term "diffused."
Not once did I declare the impounding of the
evil a necessity, yet the writer harps upon
Us necessity, and in this relation makes a
distasteful allusion to "our monasteries and
convents." The impression is disingenu
ously sought to be conveyed that I an: â¢ â !">
posed to the enforcement of the law in the
case under consideration, but it is my deter
mination to enforce the law that has lately
moved Chief Lees to act so sweepingly in
this neighborhood. The Chief has done more
in the last twenty-four hours than I ex
pected from him in six months.
The singling me out from all the Inhabi
tants of the city as the one cognizant of the
disreputable dens in the writer's nelghb< r
hood and as the competent witness to appear
before Chief Lees Is too ridiculous to notice,
and the sudden change from "coercing" to
"cajoling" the Supervisors is most laugh
able. I have full confidence in the gentle
men of the board. I believe that they are
disposed to do Justice to all. and that they
are superior to threats and cajolery.
October 27, 1898. T. CARAHER.

frotn HUo; Norwegian ship Hiawatha, i 52
viays from l,eith; bark Albert, 18 days,
and barkentine Addenda, 20 days from
