All these vesels came up the coast with
a good fair wind, but when they got off
the Golden Gate the wind fell light, and
they could make no progress. The Ad
denda attempted to sail In, but when she
grot between the heads the wii.u failed
and she began to drift. Captain Delano
was afraid bis vessel might go ashore, so
he tame 10 an anchor. In a short time
the tut; Soa King came along and towetl
him to a safer berth.
Captain Griffiths of the bark Albert got
Into trouble with the State Quarantine
Officer. While the Governor Perkins was
putting Dr. Hill aboard the Addenda Cap
tain Milestone noticed. that Captain Grif
fiths and three of his passengers were
going ashore from the Albert. He at
once gave chase and overhauled, the Al
bert's boat before she reached Powell
street wharf. The boat and passengers
were taken back to the Albert and were
told to remain there until the Quaranti*!
Officer had passed the vessel. For this
breach of the law Captain .Griffiths could
have been arrested and heavily fined, out:
the doctor did not press the matter wnen
the captain explained that he acted in
ignorance of the law.
The new ferry steamer Berkeley was
put on the run between San Francisco and ,
Oakland yesterday She took the Pied
mont's place on the route, and will Keep !
it until that vessel has been thoroughly j
overhauled. The Berkeley is a very fast i
and comfortable ferry-boat, but she will
miss the paddle-wheels when it blows a
southeaster Without these steadiers, she
will roll like a ship in a gale, and many
of the commuters will know what it is to
be seasick on the bay.
The transport Zealandla is taking on
Government stores, and will be ready to
sail for Manila on Sunday or Monday at j
the latest. . ,
The Harbor Commissioners had a lively ,
session yesterday. Commissioner Herold
started the ball rolling by moving that
two berths at Folsom street wharf be
assigned to the Alaska Packers 1 Associa- j
tion and one to the Alaska Commercial ,
Company. This Mr. Colnon objected to
on the ground that Folsom street wharf
had been built expressly for the Oceanic â 
Steamship Company, and that concern |
should be compelled to use it. Both Her
rold and Harney pointed out that the
commission was appointed to improve
and look after the shipping interests of ,
the port. The very best facilities should I
be given large companies, and if one :
wharf was better adapted to ocean trade
than another the ship owner should have
the choice. The Oceanic Steamship Com
pany found Pacific street wharf more j
suitable than Folsom street wharf and j
wanted to remain there. Other equally
large shipping concerns wanted space at
Folsom street and the board could please
everybody by granting the request of the
Alaska Packers' and Alaska Commercial
companies. The motion was carried. Then
Mr Colnon wanted the rent of Pacific
street wharf increased, and the matter |
was laid over for further consideration.
The Bibb Lumber Company asked for j
a reduction on the sum of $3850 wharfage
charged on the 1-irrber rafts that reached
pert The mat:/ was referred to the |
chief engineer and chief wharfinger. _ |
The steamer Columbia from Portland
has been unable to discharge her cargo I
of wheat and will in consequence be a |
day late in leaving port. The agents of I
the vessel complained to the commission ,
and Chief Wharfinger Root explained j
that the trouble was all caused by
grain dealers falling to remove their
wheat from the grain sheds in the regu
lation twenty days. The Commissioners
passed a resolution by which all owners
will have to pay 10 cents a ton a day for
every day over the twenty that the wheat
remains on the wharves, or until the
chief wharfinger can find a warehouse
in which to store it at the owner's ex-
P< The Norwegian ship Hiawatha, loaded
with coal, reached port last evening 152
days from Leith. Captain Jorgensen re
ports that when out about six weeks the
. coal in the main hatch became heated.
It was carefully watched for a couple
of weeks, when it became hotter and a
large portion of it had to be thrown over
board. The cargo again became heated
shortly before the arrival here. On
August 26 the vessel was struck by a
heavy sea which knocked the life boats
loose and broke the compass on the
bridge. The boats were secured without
being much damaged.
DEANE AGAIN ON THE STAND
Gives More Details of the Looting of
the Nevada Bank.
Becker and Creegan, notwithstanding
the fact that a strong and clever fight is
being waged to secure them freedom,
looked worried yesterday as Deane, their
accomplice, continued his story of the
methods used to swindle the Nevada
Bank out of $22,000. Deane has not for
gotten even the most trifling incident of
the steal, and the jury now knows all the
facts of the celebrated forgers'. It rests
with the prosecution simply to produce
sufficient corroborative evidence, and the
forgers stand an excellent chance of be
ing placed out of harm's way for the
remainder of their lives.
Deane closed his testimony on direct ex
amination about 3 yesterday afternoon
and was given into the hands of Attorney
II 1 ' ter Dunne for cross-examination. The
cross examination of the witness will
bly occupy the entire session of
court to-day and to-morrow. The prose
cution will begin its labors to build a
firm foundation to the story of the ac
complice.

LABORERS WANT THEIR WAGES
Efforts Made to Protect Men of the
Atlas Iron Works.
A short time ago the laborers employed
in the Atlas Iron Works of this city made
application to the State Labor Bureau to
assist them in collecting $"2100 which was
due them for services and which the com
pany failed to produce. A meeting was
called by the Board of Trade last Tues
day at which Patrick Noble, president,
and George Townly, secretary of the
works, were present and they agreed to
turn over to the men book accounts to
the value of $2^09. Until this time they
have neglected to keep their agreement.
The laborers now, fearing that the com
pany will make an attempt to collect the
accounts themselves to-day, have signed
over their claims to Labor Commissioner
Fitzgerald, and he will request those par
ti. ?â¢ whose names are attached to the
accounts to defer payment until matters
can be adjusted in a manner which will
protect the men from the loss of their
daily wage. No written agreement was
made, and it is not known whether the
law could force the return of the amount
collected on the book accounts should
it once pass into the company's hands.
The liabilities of the firm are $6900, and
its assets $2309.
The Botkin Case.
The Grand Jury spent yesterday after
noon in hearing testimony against Mrs.
Cordelia Botkin, charged with the murder
of Mrs. Dunning and Mrs. Deane. A
large number of witnesses were exam
ined, including Professor Thomas Price,
who analyzed the poisoned candy, buss
Harris and others having circumstantial
knowledge of the case. No conclusion
w;.~ reached, the |ury adjourning to meet
again to-day to take up the same matter.
It is expected that an immediate report
will be made to Judge Belcher in regard
to the finding.

THE SAN FBAyCTSCO CALL, FRIDAY, OCTOBER 2S, 1898.
HAVE TAKEN
THE TEACHERS'
NAME 111 YMI
Democratic Trick to
Control the Schools.
REGULAR TEACHERS' TICKET
A FATHERLESS CHILD IN THE
FIELD OF POLITICS.
No One Was Consulted on the Scheme
and No One Knows Who
Is Responsible
for It.
In an effort to grab all in sight the
; Democratic faction has turned conslder
| able attention to the School Board. It
has flooded the town with a ticket of
; School Directors, containing just a suffl
| cient number of Republicans to give it a
i non-partisan appearance, and it has made
; an unwarranted use of the names of the
j teachers to give it respectability and to
help it along.
It is a fatherless child in the field of
> politics. No one knows from where it
came nor who is responsible for its be
ing. It is in the field and that Is all
i any one knows about it. The name of
j the office where it was printed Is mlss
' ing. It is an anonymous communication
I to any one whose memory of school days
i is green enough to induce him to over
j look the lack of parentage. It is an
! old trick, and ie its own undoing.
The ticket is indorsed "Regular Teach
ers' Ticket." It is not the regular teach
ers' ticket. The teachers declare, some
openly and others in secret, that there
is no "regular teachers' ticket," and the
one so labeled is the same kind of a
political trick as appears at every elec
tion.
The teachers deny that they are in, poli
tics, as the ticket heading would lead peo
ple to infer, nor have any of them been
consulted in its formulation. To correct,
therefore, the impression conveyed by the
unwarranted use of their name, the fol
lowing protest has been circulated and
generally signed:
We, the undersigned, hereby protest against
a ticket being largely circulated among the
voters of the city and county of San Francisco.
Said ticket is headed "Regular Teachers'
Ticket," and contains the namea of twelve
men selected from the different municipal tick
ets. The teachers have not Issued and do not
countenance any such document. Respectfully
submitted,
Stanford Schoolâ B. Roper (acting principal).
A. Harnej-, J. E. Niven, E. Scholl. N. M. Levy,
C. E. lJunn, L. Rosenberg, Virginia Deal, A.
E. Little.
Franklin Grammar Schoolâ N. G. Gallagher,
Dr. Ira B. Dalziel of Golden Gate Park
Driving Association will act as grand

DEMOCRATS OF SAN
JOSE HEAR WHITE
Senator Ridicules the Single Tax,
But Declares It Is Not
an Issue.
SAN JOSE. Oct. 27.â Senator Stephen.
M. White addressed a large audience at
the Auditorium this evening. B. D. Mur
phy acted as chairman. J. L. Dryden,
fusion nominee for Railroad Commission
er, opened the speaking. He adhered to
the railroad question and accused the Re
publican party of representing railroad
corporations. Judge W. M. Conley, can
didate for Supreme Judge, followed with
a few remarks.
Senator White was then introduced. He
prefaced his remarks by saying he would
engage in no personalities. He knew
Gage personally and Maguire from boy
hood. Honesty and experience were nec
essary for the conduct of government,
and Maguire possessed both these virtues,
especially the former. The "Little Giant"
understood political affairs. His career in
the Legislature had been honorable, and
he made a good Judge. He had fully dis
charged his duty as Congressman. White
ridiculed the single tax proposition and
said it was not an issue. The railroads
were mentioned, but no reference was
made to the funding bill. The Southern
Pacific, he said, wished mightily that the
entire Republican ticket be elected, and
was working hard for it night and d;iy.
The speaker said the war revenue came
out of the people's pockets instead of
those of the corporations, and that work
ingmen who used beer and tobacco bore
the brunt of it. He denied that the Re
publlcanÃ should have the glory of the
war and said that Populists and Demo
crats also had taken part in it. He closed
with words of praise for the soldiers and
sailors who had participated in the war.
Jetties Improve Navigation.
SACRAMENTO, Oct. 27.â The jetties
that are being constructed In the river
at the Newton Shoals, near Rio Vista,
promise to accomplish much toward im
proving navigation in that locality. Al
ready there has been a perceptible cutting
away of the ahoal there by the confine
ment of the current, and Engineer Nurse
of the Public Works Commission said it
would surely disappear. This ahoal has
been an obstruction to navigation in times
of low water for the last fprty years.

STOCKHOLDERS
MAY HAVE THIS
YEAR TO THINK
Status of the Valley
Road Shares.
TIME FOR THOSE NOT IN
NEARLY ALL STOCK NOW
PLACED IN ESCROW.
Those Who Still Hold Out of the
Combination May Join Any
Time Until January
1, 1899.
Several weeks apo the stockholders
of the San Francisco and San Joaquin
Valley Railroad were invited to turn
their stock over to the trustees, accept
ing in return a guarantee that, if before
January 1, 1899, the combination had
proved successful, the shares they turned
in at fJO should be paid for at par, or $100
per share.
Most of the stockholders accepted thi3
Invitation at once, and a large percentage
of the capital stock was placed in escrow
with the Tnion Trust Company. The
Atchison, Topeka and Santa Fe Railway
Company having determined to take up
this option, for the purpose of getting a
through line to the coast in direct com
petition with the Southern Pacific, Rob
ert Watt, first vice president of the Val
ley road, was asked yesterday concern
ing the status of the few stockholders
who had not yet come into the combina
tion.
"They can come into It," he said, "any
time during the present year. The Atchl
son. Topeka and Santa Fe stands ready
to treat them ail alikeâ within, of coursej
a reasonable length of time."
GAGE TO RECEIVE
A ROUSING WELCOME
Thousands of Republicans "Will At
tend the Demonstration in
San Jose.
SAN JOSE, Oct. 27.â Henry T. Gage
will next Tuesday be given the greatest
ovation ever tendered a gubernatorial
candidate In Santa Clara County. Plans
for this greeting and welcoming of Cali
fornia's future Governor were agreed
upon to-night at a meeting of the Santa
Clara County Republican League. There
are to be two immense daylight meet-
Ings, followed by a reception. The Audi
torium and Turn Verein Hall have been
engaged and there will be speaking at
both places at the same time.
Besides Gage, Colonel E. F. Preston and
Hon. T. B. Hutchison will deliver ad
dresses. Gage will open the speaking in
the Auditorium at 3 o'clock, and at the
same time Preston will speak at Turn
Vereih Hall. Gage will then go to Turn
Verein Hall and Preston to the Auditor
ium All three speakers will address both
assemblages. By holding two meetings
all will have a chance to hear Gage. The
railroads have been asked to make a
one-half rate from Palo Alto, Gllroy,
Niles and Wrights. Special trains will be
run, returning after the reception. D. F.
McGraw, chairman of the league, states
that all farmers and orchardlsts will be
notified, and it is expected that at least
15,000 people will gather in the city and
hear Gage. _

BEGINNING THE TWENTY-SIXTH YEAR.
DERHAPS you used* to read ST. Nicholas in its early days. Do
JT you remember how you enjoyed it, how you looked forward with
eagerness to the day of issue? Twenty-five years have gone by since
the first number appeared in November, 1873, but the magazine is to-
day just what it was then, â
The Best Periodical in the World for Girls and Boys.
It has the same editor, Mary Mapes Dodge, and the same policy,â
to get the best things in literature and art that money can buy and
young folks will enjoy and thrive under.
Young people to-day like to read the Henty books, â so Mr. Henty
has written for them a story of American history which will appear as a
serial in St. Nicholas for the coming year; and.Mrs. Amelia E. Barr,
author of " Jan Vedder's Wife," will furnish a historical romance of Old
New York and Mrs. Laura E. Richards will contribute a serial; and
there are to be good things from many writers, â Mrs. Burton Harrison,
Lieut- Robert E. Peary, Lloyd Osbourne, Mrs. Charles D. Sigsbee,
(the wife of the Maine's commander). Poultney Bigelow, and others.
Gelett Burgess is to contribute a series of remarkable pictures and verse
about the " Goop Babies." Every number of St. Nicholas will be as
good as can be made.
Keep in mind the fact that the young people of to-day are just as
anxious to read ST. NICHOLAS as you used to be, â and how many of
them can you subscribe for and make happy?
The price is $j.oo a year, and the year begins with November, an especially
beautiful birthday issue. December is the Christmas Number. All dealers or
the publishers take subscriptions.

WOOL SUPPLIES FOR IS9B.
The National Association of Wool Manufac
turers has completed it* estimates oi wool
supplies in and for the United States for the
year 1898. The number of sheep in the coun
try on the Ist of April. 18DS. was 55.671.514. The
average weight of the fleece is 6.44 pounds. A
shrinkage of 61 per cent is allowed on washed
and unwashed clipped wool and 40 per cent on
washed and unwashed pulled wool. The pro
duct for I.SSS of fleece and pulled wool, washed
and in the grr-ase. is given at 266,720. 654 pounds,
equal to 111.6t>1.501 pounds scoured wool.

increase of 7,567,483 pound? over 1897. Otherwise
it is the smallest clip in many years. The

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The weather is cloudy over Washington and
Oregon and clear in the remaining portions of
the Pacific Slope. Light rain has fallen in the
western portions of Washington and Oregon
during the day.
There has been a slight but general fall in
pressure over the country west of the Rocky
Mountains.
The changes in temperature have been slight
in all districts.
The following high winds are reported to
day: Fnrt Canby, 40 miles per hour, from the
south; Portland, 28, south, and Idaho Falls,
30, south.
mion? are favorable for fair weather in
California Friday.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, Oct. 27.â The stock market
showed a continuation of yesterday's strength
to-day, and the speculative activity broadened
out considerably in addition. There was not
quite such a congestion of trading in Tobacco
and Sugar, although these stocks were the
leaders in point of activity. They were traded
in nearly all day at a level below yesterday's
rinse, though each stock advanced slightly
above yesterday's price for a brief interval.
Sugar left off with a decline of 3>i from the
highest and a loss of 1% from yesterday. To

of outside plug tobacco companies. The stock

exchanges was without "effect. There was a
large demand for quite a number of railroad
stocks, the Grangers leading, with pretty much
all the Western and Southern roads showing a
good tone. The Eastern trunk lines continued
to lag on account of the admittedly bad rate
situation in that territory which nullified the
benefit of the exceptionally large tonnage which
is being moved. St. Paul was the leader in
the railway list, and the movement of the stock
â was supposed to foreshadow a good September
statement due to-morrow.
The gross increase of that period in earnings
is known to be well on toward $600,000. and it
Is surmised that the statement will show a re
du- f 'C>n In the recent heavy fates of expendi
tures for improvements charged to operating
expenses. The September statement of the
Union Pacific, while showing p decrease of over
$44,000 in gross earnings, showed operating ex
penses so largely reduced as to leave an ln
fn surplus earnings of $112, 554. The de
layed movement of cotton now coming for
ward and the decrease of yellow fever fore
shadow good earnings for Southern and South
western railroads. The easier tone to-day of
Sterling Exchange was the direct result of the
cal .er feeling in Paris. There was a heavy de

Piiris account, which sprang from the same
cause as that which lifted exchange on Lon
don in Paris above the gold export point, name
ly, the nressure to transfer funds from Paris
to London. It is now known also that the
violent recovery In British consols was partly
due to buying for account of timid Paris capi
tal because of the domestic disquiet, and not al
together to the relief of the Anglo-French ten-
Fion over Fashoda. There was a good absorp
tion of railroad bonds to-day, and prices are
generally higher. Total sales, $3.4*0,000.

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. Oct. 27. -.The Evening Post's
London financial cablegram says:
The cheerful tone in the stock markets here
at the close yesterday was maintained to-day.
The rise was most marked in Americans and
In Kaffirs, the former closing quiet, at the
best chiefly on New York support. The one
small failure at the settlement to-day Is of no
significance, but the recovery in prices Is time-
Iv, as there are some weak spots.
* Consols closed under the best, and should
there be any renewal of political alarms there
is no way a small bear account can support
prices. 'â '".â¢' *
The best informed operators in the city do not
consider the Fashoda question as settled yet. ,
The very sharp drop in Paris exchange to-day
is partly attributed to the completion of pay
ments due from French interests in connection
with our stock exchange settlement. Russian
exchange is rising and sterling exchange m
Vienna also advanced to-day.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, Oct. 27.â Wheat started tame and
in the early part of the day had nothing to
stir it into more than moderate activity. How
ever the firmness of the cash markets in the
Northwest and a recovery at Liverpool re
sulting in Ud advance for the day Imbued the
crowd here with a desire for the buying side.
TheT accordingly pursued a bull policy during
the forenoon, but in a conservative way taking
moderate quantities on the soft spots. Eng
lish consols opened 5-16 higher and made a fur
ther Vi per cent advance, retaining all of it
at the close. This peaceful Indication about
balanced smaller domestic receipts and the
closing firmness of the Liverpool market. The
bulls weakened near the end and bears per
sisted in selling on all hard spots. December
left off at %'S 1 /ic decline on the day.
Late receipts and continued complaints of the
poor condition resulting from the recent damp
weather strengthened corn. Prices showed a
good advance the greater part of the day, but
near jihe end weakness developed in sympathy
with wheat and th? advance was nearly all
lost December closing with a gain of %c.
Oats were quiet, prices being sustained by the
Etrencth in corn and a good cash demand.
May closed unchanged.
The heavy run of hogs was a depressing fea
ture in provisions and more than balanced the
excellence of the demand. The market ruled
dull throughout and the tendency of prices
again was slightly downward. Pork closed
71..-.C lower, lard lost 5c and ribs 7V4c lower.

CALIFORNIA RAISINS IN NEW . YORK.
NEW Y*ORK. Oct. 27.â The Journal of Com
merce under this date says: The situation in
the market for California raisins Is very unsat-
isfactoryto packers' and commission merchants'
jobbers. The first named are unable because
of poor drying weather to deliver goods con
tracted for on time, and consequently -the as
sociation rules will have to stand the difference
between prices prevailing prior to October 15
and those which went into force on that date.
Delay in deliveries has been the cause of con
siderable embarrassment to commission mer-:
chants at this end. They have been unable
to fully make good their contracts with job
bers, and the latter, who need goods very
much, are dissatisfied and uncertain as to the
future. While goods contracted for are being 1
delayed some buyers are obliged to go into the
market and make purchases in order to
promply meet the requirements of their trade.
The demand for California raisins has not been
more than a third of the average since the
season opened. No raisins are reported to have
been sold to Eastern markets, at least not
east of the Allefrhanies, ;.t prices which took
effect on October 15. but trade In this section
seems to very much want to know why de
liveries on contracts maile prior to that date
have not been made as fully as was expected
they would be.
Eastern handlers of raisins generally are of
the opinion that consolidation of the California
industry would be a good thing. for everybody
concerned, and are disposed to make every al
lowance for hitches In carrying out what they
concede Is an experiment, but are Inclined to
be severe in criticisms of the management of
the business as conducted by the present asso
ciation.

SHEEPSKINS IN LONDON.
LONDON, Oct. 27.â A sale of sheepskins was
held here to-day. The offerings amounted to
4400 bales in good condition. There was a fair
attendance, but the buying- was irregular,
about 1100 bales being left over. The Continent
bought sparingly. The home trade took the
bulk of the offerings after it was seen that
buyers were indisposed to pay the prices
named. The unsettled wool market has a de
presalng effect. Merinos were a farthing to
%d lower and cross breda a farthing to '^d
lower than the last sale.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Prices for mining stocks were lower again,
with a corresponding decrease in sales.
Local securities were more active, and prices
were rather irregular, though there were no
variations worthy of note.
An exchange says: "The Anaconda Copper
Mining Company of Montana has declared a
dividend of $1 25 per share, payable November
1. The total amount of the dividend is $1,500,
000 on 1,200,000 shares of capital stock. The
last previous dividend was paid in May and
was of the same amount. The total of $1,500,000
is the largest dividend now being paid by any
mining company in the United States. The
total dividends paid by the Anaconda to date
amount to $8, 250,000."
The Pacific Lighting Company has declared
the usually monthly dividend of 40 cents per
share, payable November 5.
The Alaska-Treadwell Gold Mining Com
pany will pay a regular quarterly dividend
of 75 cents per share to-day.
The Smuggler Mining Company of Colorado
paid a dividend of 1 cent per share, amount-
Ing to $10,000, on October 15.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographic
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding 1
lights, dnng-ers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory, Mare Island. Cal.
A "notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, is published
In the"mornlng papers the following day.
CHAS. P. WELCH.
Knsign (retired), U. S. N., in charge.

NO PARK YET
CREATED AT
DEATH'S CURVE
Attorneys Examine a
Franchise.
MILLER'S HEIRS MAY SUE
GATES DESIRED AT FIRST AND
"WEBSTER.
Great Danger to Foot Passengers
Who Are Not Aware of the
Recent Change of
Track.
Oakland Office San Irancisco Call,
90S Broadway, Oct. 27.
Some attorneys called at the City
Clerk's office to-day and examined the j
ordinance under which a franchise was
granted to the Southern Pacific Company
to lay its Webster-street tracks across!
the sidewalk near Second street, and ]
build a curve so as to run onto the new
Harrison-street drawbridge. The pur
pose of such examination Is undoubtedly
preparatory to bringing a 6utt for dam
ages, as there is a clause in the ordi
oance which has not been obeyed. When
the railroad company applied for the
franchise from Webster to Harrison |
there was much opposition In the Council j
owing to the arguments of Mr. Watkin
son and others that the franchise would
establish another "death curve." The
railroad company overcame this objec
tion by stating that it had purchased the
â whole of the block on which the curve
was to cross, and that it would till it in
and make a public park of the remainder
and that this park should be kept :"
all obstructions, so as to prevent loss of
life because of foot passengers not being
able to see the train.
The new track has been in operation '
some weeks and ihe park has not yet
been created, while at one corner of the
lot there are some shanties which it was '
understood were to be pulled down. These
chanties are on the opposite- corner from
where Othmar Miller was killed last
Tuesday evening. A cement sidewalk haa
leeil In u:>e at this point for some years,
and for twenty years the narrow-gauge i
train has gone down Webster street and
straight over Webster-street bridge. It :
ims across the sidewalk, and resi- 1
dents In that neighborhood think that
.there should be automatic gates across
the' sidewalk, inasmuch as they have hiren
accustomed to use it for so long and
there are many who do not yet under-
Btand that the train switches off its old
track at that point and crosses the block
diagonally. The danger is almost as great
When the- train switches from First street
across the sidewalks onto Webster street j
going north. It was on a northern trip
that Miller was killed. There is a nag
man at the point where the accident oc
curred, but after dark it is impossible for i
a stranger to distinguish his light, espe- j
cially as there are many signal lamps In
that vicinity.
It is thought that the ordinance was be-
Ir.g examined by some one interested in
MfHer's death.
MAHONEY ARRESTED.
Though Twice Looked Into by the
Grand Jury the District Attor
ney Advises Dismissal.
OAKLAND, Oct. 27.â William Mahoney,
who was arrested last night on a charge
of perjury preferred by Fred Murdoch:,
ex-Assistant Chief of the Fire Depart
ment, has been released on $30000 bonds.
Mahoney is particularly charged with
having illegally registered last July, he
having sworn to his age as 22 years, when,
as Murdock claims, he is not 21.
The case is a most peculiar one, since
the Grand Jury investigated Mahoney
twice and believing him of age had
dropped the matter. Furthermore, it ap-
Bears that the complaipant went to the
'istrict Attorney's office yesterday to se
cure a warrant, and being refused, ap
peared before Justice of the Peace Law
rence and' finally obtained a warrant.
Mahoney was arraigned before Justice
Lawrence this morning. He had no coun
eel and refused to plead. On the other
hand Deputy District Attorney A. A.
Moore Jr. moved for a dismissal on the
ground that the complaint had no foun
dation upon which a conviction could be
Efccured. Justice Lawrence refused to
grant this, saying he did not wish to take
snap Judgment on the complaining wit
ness, who, he believed, should be given
a chance to be heard.
Attorney Moore then asked the court
whether he was acting on the advice of
Murdock or on the advice of the Dis
trict Attorney's office. The court replied
that he was not taking Murdock's advice,
and after more words the case was set for
next Saturday morning.
Mahoney's arrest is the sequel of a
Fire Department row.

Indications That the Estate of Han-
nah Butler Will Be Litigated.
OAKLAND, Oct. 27.â There is likely to '

Butler, who died in this city on Septem- j

queathing all of her estate to friends and
relatives, except $5, which is left to her
husband. Patrick Butler, aged 75 years, ]
she "believing that he has enough money
and plenty of property of his own."
There are several peculiarities about
the affairs of decedent which bespeak a
woman of considerable eccentrcity. Her |
estate consists for the most part of cash. |
She has 15261 30 deposited in the Hibernia '
Savings and Loan Society under the name
of Clara White; $2000 in La Societie Fran
caiae under the name of Mrs. A. McCar
thy: $2000 in the- Humboldt Savings and
Loan Society under the name of Hannah
McCarthy, besides a house and lot at to! 0
Kirkham street.
By the terms of the will the house and
lot are bequeathed to Mary Hook of this !
city; Herman and John Sou pal re of Oak- !
land and San Rafael respectively are each
to receive $2500; John Hook of Omaha.
Neb., $2000, and Hannah Leary of Oakland,
$1000.
Rev. Brother Michael of h n Rafael is
named in the will as exp< a , r , and tes
tator "appoints Brother _K*-iel guardian
Â«f John and Herman Soupaire."
Death Was by Asphyxiation.
OAKLAND, O<t. 27. â Coroner Raldwin
and a jury this morning inquired into the
death of John Campbell at Llvermore. Ine
evidence showed that decedent and A. A.
Fargo, the latter being foreman of tne
"Wallace Everson vineyard, near Liver
more, went to a wine tank for the pur
pose of giving the same its periodical
cleaning. Campbell entered first, and
Fargo was to have followed. Campbell
made an effort to get out again, when
Fargo, jrrabliing him, tried to pull him
out, but without avail. Decedent dropped
back into the tank and \v;)s asphyxiated
by the gases. A verdict of death by as
phyxiation was rendered.
Charged With Murderous Assault.
OAKLAND, Oct. 27.â Charles Degreef,
a sailor, was brought to the County oail
about noon to-day charged with assault
to murder Howard Davis. J?oUi men re-
Bide in San Francisco, and were on a
leave of absence. They had Imbibed free
ly of Alameda whisky, and during a dis
pute on High street, in the Enclnal City,
Degreef, it is charged, pulled a weapon
and tried to kill his opponent, when by
standers prevented further harm.

GRAND JURY
AT LAST FILES
ITS REPORT
Indulges in Usual
Criticism.
CHURCH IS COMMENDED
COUNTY HOSPITAL SAID TO BE
A FIRST-CLASS PLi-CE.
The Late Frank P. Thwaite Was Paid
Vast Sums of Money on
Alleged Raised
Orders.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, Oct. 27.
The long-expected, report of the Grand
Jury was filed this afternoon, and was a
great disappointment to those who ex
pected an interesting document contain
ing- facts. Instead it dealt with a host of
generalities, and contained nothing that
has not from time to time been published
during ihe ten months' investigation of
the body.
The report commends the management
of the County Jail and City Prison. It
pays a high tribute to the management of
the County Infirmary, of which Super
visor Will H. Church is the chairman.
The report says:
The hallways and rooms throughout the
buildings were well ventilated and thoroughly
clean, the food abundant and of good quality,
everything Indicating proper discipline and
commendable attention to the wants and com
fort of tfce inmates.
Seeing so much to commend In this Institu
tion, it is with some reluctance that we call
attention to the large increase in expenditures
fur 1537 over the preceding- year, viz., $14,401 89.
It Is true that a portion of this was spent
on the new reservoir, pipe line, fire protection
plant, new buildings, etc., permanent improve
ments that possibly could have been deferred
to a more convenient season, but, nevertheless,
very necessary to the completeness of a first
class Infirmary, -which this certainly Is.
In referring to the expenses of the hos
pital â  the following- typical sentence oc

The expert for the Grand Jury went
back to the records of 1892, and from his
investigation it appears that during the
past six years Frank P. Thwaites the of
ficial stenographer, who died some
months ago. raiser! his orders on the
county treasury until he defrauded the
county of over $20,000.
The manner in which Thwaites is al
leged to have worked is thus described
by .the expert:
After working for one day Thwaites would
present an order for his pay, amounting to $10,
make the affidavit out and the Justice would
sign the order; the amount would be entered
in figures only, but the blank space Just over
the Justices signature where the amount
Fhould be filled in in writing he purposely left
vacant and the Justice seldom if ever paid any
attention to It. Here. then, was an oppor
tunity; he would make a ftsjure (say 4) out of
the 1; that would make four days instead of
one; then change In like manner the $10 to $40
and insert the words "forty" and "00" in
writing in the blank space over the Justice's
signature and the order was ready to be
cashed. _________
A CLEW TO GOETZ.
The Missing Stanford Student Was
Seen Sunday by an Alameda
Policeman.
ALAMEDA, Oct. 27.â The Alameda Po
lice Department is making no effort to
locate Adolph Goetz, the missing Stan
ford student, who was last seen In this
city. They say they have not been offi
cially notified of his disappearance, and
without such official notification do not
feel called upon to volunteer their ser
vices In the search for the missing man.
Mrs. A. H. Clark, of 1417 Sherman
street, at whose home Goetz was visiting
at the time he disappeared, called at the
police station Sunday afternoon with a
picture of the young man. She told the
officer in charge that the photograph was
that of a friend who had disappeared,
but she refused to give his name. She
left the picture, however, and it remained
at police- headquarters until the following
day when she sent for it.
A possible clew to the missing student
is furnished by Officer Kamp. He saw
the picture, and states that late Sunday
afternoon he saw a young man who very
much resembled the photograph walking
along Park street in the direction of the
tidal canal.
"Not knowing the young fellow's name,
or the name of the lady who had report
ed him missing. I was in no position to
stop him, as he was minding his own
business and disturbing no one," said the
policeman. ,
I'u to this evening nothing further had
been heard of the young man. Mrs.
("lark has received Information from Palo
Alto to the effect that when Qoetz left
the university Saturday he had over $60
in his possession. How much of this he
had left when he reached Mrs. Clark's
home is not known, as he did not display
any money there and did not discuss his
financial affairs.

THE SAN FRAXCISCO CALL, FBIDAY, OCTOBER 28, IC9B.1 C 9B.
IS IN FEAR i
, OF DEATH IN
HER HOI
Her Husband Used
Knives and Razors.
AN ABUSED WIFE'S STORY
MRS. SPENCER FREE AFTER
TWENTY-ONE YEARS.
Her Once Penitent Husba- d Wrote
Threatening Letter, but Did
Not Carry Out His
Intention.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Oct. 27.
Margaret F. Spencer is a free woman;
that is to say, after twenty-one years
of wedded life with Lysander Spencer
Judge Greene has granted her a decree
of divorce.
There have been some sensational di
vorce stories lately told in the Alameda
courts, but none more heartrending than
the tale of marital unhappinesa and cru
elty related by Mrs. Spencer. Twenty
one years ago they were married. Before
their wedding- Mrs. Spencer discovered
that her husband cultivated habits not
tending toward happiness. It was partly
with a desire to reform him that she
consented to become his wife. For years
he gradually fell from grace, but did
nothing to cause an open breach. Some
years ago his mother-in-law died, and
Spencer a few days after the burial en
tered the house and wanted to know "if
the racket was all over." Mrs. Spencer
resented such language referring to the
burial of her mother, and there was a
quarrel. According to the testimony
Spencer dragged his wi/e to the rear of
the house, raised a knife in anger and
suddenly, becoming smitten with grief,
cried out "Maggie, I can't kill you. He
satisfied his anger on that occasion by
cutting the arteries of his own wrist.
Mrs. Spencer said from that day she
has been in constant dread for her life,
and that on many occasions she has frus
trated her husband's plans. Tn May. 1897,
Spencer was attacked with Klondicitis
and he asked his wife for money with
which to go north. She declined to give
him any and he became mad, and, accord
ing to Mrs. Spencer, told her her time
had come. This episode was terminated by
a visit from another tenant In the house
who had heard the disturbance. Mrs.
Spencer fled from home, and her husband,
prostrate with grief at their unhapplness,
wrote his wife a note in which he said:
"I will leave this cold, cold world by
my own hand. Life is a failure for me.
No God. no wife, no fireside. This is the
last news from me. The next time you
hear from me will be in happy, blessed
death. This is not idle talk, for you Will
find me dead in the house of your friend."
Apparently the talk was idle, for hus
band and wife lived together until he
handled a razor in an unbecoming man
ner and Mrs. Spencer departed.
After hearing the little story Judge
Greene decided that Mrs. Spencer might
be divorced from her husband.
A NITROGLYCERIN
TANK EXPLODES
THREE MEN NARROWLY ES
CAPE INSTANT DEATH.
Combustible Chemicals Play Havoc
in the Factory of the California
Vigorit Powder Company.
BERKELEY, Oct. 27.â A storage tank at
the California Vigorit Powder Works at
Point Isabel exploded about 11:30 o'clock
this morning. Had the accident hap
pened a minute earlier the event would
have turned into a tragedy, bringing to
three men an instant death.
The explosion occurred in what Is
known as the recovery building, where
nitric and sulphuric acids are separated
from nitroglycerln. It appears that the
glycerin In the receiver became over
heated, either spontaneously or from the
presence of a greater quantity of the ma
terial than the volume of the receiver
will allow, and the big tank blew up with
out a moment's warning.
The shock was distinctly felt in Berke
ley, a distance of four miles. The prin
cipal damage was confined chiefly to the
scene of the accident, although several
windows were broken In some of the sur
rounding houses. The magazine and
packing houses standing about a hun
dred feet away were left unharmed.
The escape of the three men belonging
to the company was almost miraculous.
Robert Burcher. in charge of the recov
ery building, together with John Cain
and Superintendent P. G. Gallagher, had
just stepped out of the building when ihe
upheaval came. They were in one of the
sheds near by, and escaped without in
jury.
In explaining the cause of the acci
dent Frank J. Gallagher, one of the offi
cers of the company, said: "I feel sure
that the glycerin in the tank became
heated spontaneously, for I don't see how
the tank could have gone up in any other
way. This morning 'here was not more
than twenty pounds of glycerin In it,
but even that was sufficient to cause this
wreck. Even with the greatest care in
this work an explosion sometimes hap
pens."
FELL THIRTY FEET
THROUGH A SKYLIGHT
ARTHUR HOLMES' MIRACULOUS
ESCAPE FROM DEATH.
Dropped From the lloof of the Oak
land Iron Works, Sustaining a
Cerebral Contusion.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
SOB Broadway, Oct. 27.
Arthur Holmes, aged 15 years, residing
with his parents at 213 Third street, fell
through a skylight in the roof of the Oak
land Iron Works to-night to the floor be
low, a distance of nearly thirty feet. He
miraculously escaped death.
There is a cupola on the roof In which
iron is melted, and to prevent sparks ig
niting the timbers about it 1b necessary
to dampen the surface in its immediate
vicinity. Holmes was engaged in this
latter work when it was quitting time, 6
o'clock. The surface of the roof was wet.
He in some manner lost his hold, sliding
until he reached the skylight, when he
crashed through the panes and fell upon
the floor in the machine shop below.
He was picked up unconscious and
taken to the Receiving Hospital, where
he was treated for a severe 6calp wound.
So far as could be determined to-night he
sustained no other injuries, though there
is little doubt he is injured internally.
When Holmes' body crashed through
i the skylight most of the employes at the
works had left for the day. Had not the
noise of the breaking panes attracted the
attention of a man, who returned to in
vestigate, the boy would have remained
there all night unattended.
Marriage Without a Home.
OAKLAND, Oct. 27.â Leah E. Stutt
wants to be divorced. She has been mar
ried nearly a year, but her husband has
no idea of giving her a home. In No
vember, 1597, ehe sayÃ In her complaint,
the wedding took place, and the first piece
of information that the husband gave his
wife was that he was unable to supply
a home, and he kindly advised her to live
with her parents. The bride, who was
17 years old, took her husband's advice.
She went home and she is still at home.
She declares that her husband has never
since offered to give her a home or to
provide any support for herself and their
little child. Mr. Stutt is earning $100 a
month and the young wife thinks she is
entitled to one-half of this amount.
His Head Split Open.
OAKLAND, Oct. 27.â G. E. Garrison, a

morning, and was seriously injured. His

Barely Escaped Death.
ALAMEDA, Oct. 27.â Ward Wells, the
eight-year-old son of C. B. Wells of the
Hawaiian Islands, nearly lost his life last
evening by falling from the platform of
a narrow gauge train. The little fellow
was visiting friends in Alameda and took
the 5 o'clock train at Chestnut street sta
tion to ride as far as Morton street. He
stood on the platform and lost his bal
ance by the jolting of the cars. He fell,
striking the ground within six inches of
the rails, but fortunately rolled away
from the track. His face was severely
bruised and cut, one eye was badly in
jured and he suffered a slight concussion
of the brain.

SEVEN PINES CIRCLE
HAS A CAMPFIRE
CHARLES L. PATTON'S PORTRAIT
EVOKES APPLAUSE.
A Pretty Drill by Ladies of the Circle
and a Soldier's Old Time
Camp Feast.
There was a laree audience in B'r.ai
B'rith Hall last night to enjoy the pro
gramme prepared by the members of
Seven Pines Circle, Ladies of the G. A.
R., for thtlr twelfth annual camp fire.
There was a profusion of patriotic deco
rations and on the walls were the names
of many of the prominent candidates on
the Republican tickets.
Mrs. James H. Whitesides presided, and
after an overture of patriotic airs, she
introduced Dr. Danziger, who delivered
an address on the noble wurk that thÂ«
ladies of the order are engaged in. MtM
Annie Corrigan gave two recitations; Mr.
Wilson and Mrs. Ida L^roi amused the
audience with a rendition in character of
"The Chinee and the Coon." after which
there were vocal (selections by a quintet
of the Fifty-first lowa Volunteers.
As the vocalists retired a veteran ap
peared on the stage, placed a staff on the
center and without uttering a word, hung
on it 'a white silken banner, surrounded
with red white and blue. In the center
was a tine likeness of Charles L. Patton,
the Republican candidate for Mayor, ana
above it was painted in letters of gold
his name and the office to which he win
be elected. This silent appeal produced a
marked effect on those who gazed at the
stage and with one accord there was a
tremendous burst of applause, showing
that the great majority was in accord
with the candidacy of Mr. Patton.
This incident was followed by a drill
by the following named ladies of the cir
cle, who were attired in a neat uniform
in which the red, white and blue predom
inated: Anne Eyers, president of the cir
cle; Jeannette Harvey, Lizzie Isolle,
Laura Oblinger, Abble Powis, Annie i.
Black Lulu Watrou, Annie Schweitzer,
Ella Tate, Lizzie Houtz, Sadie Pierpont,
Marchie Wenk, Rosie Perry, Louise Carr,
Jennie Martin and Julia Duhem. This
corps was put through a number of evolu
tions by J. E. Tower of the Fifty-first
lowa. A pretty feature of the drill was
the formation in living letters of the ab
breviation of the name of the order, L.
of T G. A. R. The ladies were rewarded
by long and loud applause.
After the drill there was dancing and
an old-fashioned camp fire supper, con
sisting of soldiers' fare.
CHAMPIONSHIP REGATTA.
Oarsmen Preparing for a Day of Ex
citing Sport at Old Long Bridge.
The regatta committee of the Pacific
Athletic Association is hard at work pre
paring for the championship races to bÂ«j
held at Long Bridge on Sunday, Novem
ber 6. A meeting was held Wednesday
night, at which the following members of
the board of nTanagera were present. W. C. ,
Espey (chairman), J. R. Bockman (sec- ;
retary), J. W. Morton, M. Breen, S. J. [
Pembroke and A. P. Rothkopf. The re
gatta will be on a large scale, and great
preparations are being made for it. A
band of music will be engaged, and will
play throughout the day. J. R. Bock
man, M. Breen and S. J. Pembroke were
appointed a committee to look after the
musical part of the programme. A stand
will be erected on the bridge, from which
candidates for office who have donated
subscriptions to the regatta funds may
address the people. Saloon privileges
have been granted to Louis yon Harten,
but no gambling or poolselling will be
permitted, the intention being to make the
event a purely amateur one.
E. Flanders will set the stakes and
supply the launch for the committee. The
names of candidates who have given sub
scriptions to the regatta will be painted
upon a banner, the name of only one
candidate for each office being accepted
by the committee. The following candi
dates have already contributed: Julius j
Kahn, candidate for Congress; John D. '
Siebe, for Assessor; Henry S. Martin, for
Sheriff; Charles F. Curry, for Secretary
of State: John H. Grady, for Auditor; Ed
H Kalben. William H. Phelps, Howard
Black, Ed Holland, Dr. E. C. Perrault
and Ed I. Sheehan for Supervisors, and
C. F. Kenneally for Assemblyman of the
Twenty-ninth District. A circular letter
mentioning all the candidates who have
contributed will be sent to every member j
of the rowing clubs on the bay, and to
all persons upon the invitation lists of the
clubs.
The regatta promises to be very inter
esting and to furnish several exciting con
tests. It is highly probable that the re
sults of the regatta recently held on Rus
sian River will, in several instances, be
reversed.

PROCLAMATION.
STATE OF CALIFORNIA,
EXECUTIVE DEPARTMENT,
SACRAMENTO. July 30th. 1898.
WHEREAS. The Legislature of the State of
California, at its thirty-second session begin-
ning on the fourth day of January, A. D. 1897.
two-thirds of all the members elected to each
of the two houses of said Legislature voting
In favor thereof, proposed the following de-
scribed amendments to the Constitution or the
State of California, to-wlt: :
AMENDMENT NUMBER ONE.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
41.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California an amendment to section
eighteen of article eleven of the Constitu-
tion, in relation to revenue and taxation, by
which it is proposed to amend said section to
read as follows:
Section 18. No county, city, town, township,
board of education or school district shall in-
cur any indebtedness or liability in any man-
ner or for any purpose exceeding In any year
the Income and revenue provided for It â  Â£ Â°
such year, without the assent of two-tnlras
of the qualified electors thereof, voting at an
election to be held for that purpose, nor un-
less before, or at the time of incurring such
Indebtedness, provision shall be made for the
collection of an annual tax sufficient to pay
the interest on such indebtedness as it falls
due and also provision to constitute a sinking
fund for the payment of the principal thereof
on or before maturity, which shall not exceed
forty years from the time of contracting, the
same; provided, however, that the City and
County of San Francisco may at any time pay
the unpaid claims with interest thereon for
materials furnished to and work done for said
city and county during the forty-third ana
forty-fourth fiscal years, out of the income and
revenue of any succeeding year or years: pro-
vit!?d. that any and all claims for making, re-
pairing, altering or for any work done upon or
for any material furnished for any street, lane,
alley, court, place or sidewalk, or for the con-
struction of any sewer or sewers In said city
and county are hereby excepted from the pro-
visions of this section; and in determining any
claim permitted to be paid by this section, no
statute of limitations shall apply in any man-
ner- and provided further, that the City of
Vallejo. In Solano County, may pay its exist-
ing indebtedness incurred in the construction
of its water works, whenever two-thirds of the
electors thereof voting at an election held for
that purpose shall so decide. Any indebted-
ness or liability incurred contrary to this pro-
vision, with the exception hereinbefore recited,
shall be void.

port.
The First and Second Battalions of the
Twentieth Kansas Regiment made their
final exit from the Presidio reservation
at 10 o'clock yesterday. Colonel Funston,
the newly made benedict, was In com
mand. The line of march was down Lom
bard street and thence direct to pier ...
â¢where the Indiana was tied up. A large
crowd congregated at the wharf to bid
the soldiers good-by and withness their
embarkation. About 3 o'clock in the
afternoon Colonel Funston called at de
partment headquarters to receive his final
Instructions from General Merriam. it
was an hour later before the transport
weighed her anchor and began her long
voyage to Manila.
This morning the same scenes will be
repeated at the post of the Presidiu. Ihe
First and Third Battalions of the First
Washington Regiment, Colonel John
Wholley commanding, will go on board the
Ohio, which will sail this evening ; To
morrow the companies of the Ki^Kih
California Regiment, now at Fort P< int.
will come into the Presidio to do garri-
Bon duty. They will be quartered in the
post and succeed to the duties formerly
performed by the Washington soldiers.
The latter were formally relieved irom
du'tv at the post by an order publisluu at
â  their final parade yesterday afternoon.
The formal assignment of troups to the
ZeaJandia has not been made, but it is
practically settled that seven companies
of the First Tennessee Regiment will go
on this vessel. On the Newport will be
the Third Battalion of the Kansas, the
remainder of the California Heavy Artil
lery and General Miller and his staff.
The third transport that will be secured
â will be either the City of Pueblo or the
Morgan City. General Merriam will to
day communicate with the War Depart
ment relative to Including the Wyoming
Light Battery and the Nevada Cavalry
among the expeditionary forces.
Second Lieutenant W. I. Hinckley, First
Washington, will not sail with his regi

main behind for the purpose of accom

first lowa Regiment to the Pennsylvania
shows that the organization is exactly

A PROBLEM FOR THE JUDGE.
He Must Decide Who Is the Owner
of a Goat and Its Kid.
A most perplexing problem will be sub
mitted to Judge Joaehimsen this morning
and much Interest Is manfested as to the
result of his decision. Lawyers and citi
zens are equally interested and when the
case i 3 heard there will be a crowded
courtroom.
Yesterday morning Mrs. Margaret Ham
ilton, a lady SO years of age, living on
Twenty-second and Tennessee streets, ob
tained a search warrant from Assistant
Prosecuting Attorney Drury of Judge
Joachimsen's court for the recovery of
a goat which had been stolen from her
eighteen months ago. Since then she had
been constantly searching for the goat
and it was only Wednesday that she dis
covered her pet In the yard of John
Dwyer, on the Potrero hill, near Sixteenth
atreet.
What pleased her more than anything
else was to find that a kid was disporting
â¢itself beside the goat as she had looked
forward to the advent of the kid with
feelings of joy, as she longed to make a
particular pet of It. She made a demand
upon Dwyer for the return of the goat
and the kid, but Dwyer denied that either
belonged to her.
She told her story to Drury and he
thought the scare)] warrant should In
clude the kid as well as the goat, but it
would rest with the Judge to determine
whether Dwyer could lay claim to the
kid. She said she was prepared to swear
that the goat and kid belonged to her, but
Dwyer denies that the goat .ever was hers
and therefore both the goat and kid are
his property.
Policeman C. M.Barnes went with Mrs.
Hamilton to the Dwyer homestead and
she pointed out the goat, which Barnes
seized in virtue of the search warrant.
The kid could not be seen and Barnes had
to leave without it. He took the goat to
the City Prison, where its appearance
caused the greatest consternation. Ser
geant Lindheimer objected to have any
thing to <lo with the goat. "What do you
think 1 am, " he roared at Barnes. '"'Do
you think I am the poundkeeper? Take
ft away. I don't care what you do with
it." This In answer to a question of
Barnes, who seemed perplexed to know
what to do. Finally It was arranged that
the goat should be taken to Westphal's
stable; 3 to await the decision of the Judge
this morning.

WHICH ONE IS THE HEIR?
The Alcazar Leading^Man and His Counterfeit Pre
sentment Are Busy Looking Up an
English Inheritance.
'ERNEST WILUA/v\
HASTINGS
ERNEST HASTINGS, the leading man of the Alcazar Theater, has
met his double, who not only shares with him feature for feature
but name for name. The unadulterated name of the actor is William
Ernest Hastings, and of his counterfeit presentment Ernest William Hast
ings. The leading man, however, in deference to his good square chin
in boyhood days dropped the William and Is commonly known by his mid
dle name, Ernest.
Ernest William Hastings, accompanied by his legal adviser, is at pres
ent in this city endeavoring to trace up relatives In the hope of proving
heirshlp to Â£22.600 left by the death of an aunt in Lancashire, England, to
Ernest Hastings of New York. The Alcazar actor's home Is In New York,
and It seems that Ernest William Hastings also lived there. In the will
there was no middle initial given, but attached to it was an unmounted
photograph which would answer for either party owing to the striking re
semblance. Actor Hastings says that for some months prior to coming out
here to till the Alcazar engagement he was visited by a relative who
was on his way to Europe to spend the summer with relatives there.
Hastings had just received proofs of some photos of a sitting he had
taken, and at the request o*f his relative gave him one of them. That party
has not yet returned home, neither has Ernest hoard from either him or
other of his relatives in some time. He says he has relatives in Canada,
also some living in Southampton and Liverpool, but knew of none resid
ing in Lancashire.
Hastings knew nothing of his "double," or that any property had been
left until he was approached yesterday by the attorney for Ernest Wil
liam Hastings, who, thinking that he might be a relative, could shed
some light upon his pedigree. The astonishment of the lawyer when
meeting the very likeness of his client can only be imagined. By appoint
ment both gentlemen met, but Ernest William Hastings could disclose
but few facts, as he knew but little of his people. However, he remem
bered hearing when a boy his father say he had relatives living In Eu
rope, and that some day he might be a wealthy man. He said he sent
his photograph to the authorities in several of the leading European cit
ies, asking them if they could trace up his people. Since then he has re
ceived no reply, until some ten days ago there was mailed to him a copy
of a weekly paper published In London, bearing- a cut of the will and his
photograph.
The Alcazar's leading man has dispatched a letter to an uncle of his
living in Brooklyn, directing him to Investigate at once whether or not he
1b the rightful claimant to the $112,500 now awaiting an owner in England.

TO BUILD A CANAL
ACROSS NICARAGUA
The Above Government Grants a
Concession to Chicag-o and New
York Capitalists.
MANAGUA, Nicaragua, Oct. 27.â Edwin
F. Cragln, the Chicago engineer and con
tractor, and Edward Eyere and Frank S.
Washburn of New York City, have made
an agreement with President Zelaya to
construct an interoceanic canal. Under
the terms of the concession they are au
thorized to negotiate with the Maritime
Canal Company, whose concession will
elapse next October. The"*fonn of agree
ment guarantees a release from the in
cubus of the old company while making
satisfactory arrangements with all par
ties. Thee oncessionaires represent ade
quate capital, which with efficient engi
neers and contractors, assures a prompt
beginning and an early completion of the
canal, in contrast to the Inactivity of the
Maritime Company for the last ten years.
The option requires the concessionaires
to commence work immediately after the
expiration of the existing concession in
October next. They must deposit SHiO.wO
in gold in the Nicaraguan Treasury as a
guarantee on signing the option, and
$400,000 more within three months after.
They must organize a company within
six months, begin the excavation of the
canal within twenty-six months, open up
some transit route across Nicaragua
within three years and complete the canal
within ten years. The option provides
also that the concessionaires shall give
Nicaragua S per cent of the stock and
net dividends pro rnta annually for nine
ty-nine years' traffic, dividing the net
dividends equally with Nicaragua. Sev
eral fines of $100,000 each are mentioned in
the event of non-compliance with the pro
visions specified.
The option is now before the Nicaraguan
Congress.

CHILDS DIES A SUICIDE.
Playwright Takes Poison and Sends
a Bullet Into His Brain.
PHILADELPHIA, Oct. 27.â Nathaniel
Childs, press agent of the "Way Down
East" Company, which Is playing at a
local theater, died a suicide to-day at the
Jefferson Hospital. He swallowed forty
five grains or morphine, after which he
shot himself in the head. Mr. Childs
graduated from Harvard in 1819, and for
several years he was editor of the Boston
Traveler. He entered the theatrical pro
fession In 1876 and wrote a number of
plays. At one time he was advance agent
for Henry Irving, and for ten years acted
In the same capacity for Abbey, Schoffel
& Grau.
The failure of the play, "Little Miss No
body." In December last, in which he was
interested, made him very despondent,
and he began to drink. He was about 50
years of age.

LOOKING FOR
t POISON IN
THE SUPS
Chemist Green Begins
His Analysis.
SEEKING FOR PTOMAINES
CURIOUS PROCESSES FOLLOWED
IN THE WORK.
An Investigation Fraught With
Interest and Important Results
to One Branch of
Science.
The disreputable Butehertown shrimps,
after enjoying a long period of immunity
at the hands of the law, are in serious
trouble.
Yesterday morning City Chemist Frank
Green, acting under special orders from
the San Francisco Board of Health, sent
two deputies to the slaughter-house
â wharves, armed with long cane poles, sea
grass lines and sharp hooks, to cap
ture thirty or more of the swarm of dan
gerous little wretches for analytical pur
poses. The deputies' efforts were crowned
with success and within a short time they
wended their way toward the city build
ings with the required number of shrimps.
To-day Professor Green will begin sci
entific operations upon them, and by the
time he gets throutrh there will be very
little left of these good-for-nothing
shrimps, except, possibly, a few ounces
of poison, a pot of sulphuretted hydrogen
and some unsavory memories.
For a long period of time fishermen
plying their trade in the waters of the
bay in the vicinity of the Butchertown
wharves have complained of violent
stomachic pains, necessitating frequent
calls for the doctor and heavy medical
bills, thereby entailing financial discom
"fort as well as physical suffering. As
the pains usually followed the eating of
Butchertown shrimps all theories of dys
pepsia were soon swept to the winds by
the suspicion, which soon ripened into
belief, that the shrimps were poisonous.
Nobody thought for a minute that the
fish had resorted to the dissemination of
poison with malice aforethought, but the
fact remained that shrimps in other por
tions of the bay were palatable to the
taste and wholesome for the digestion.
Finally the fishermen complained to the
Board of Health about the matter and
Dr. Gallwey made an investigation, re
porting tha"t he was led to believe that
the Butchertown shrimps were highly sea
soned with poisonous ptomaines. He ad
vised that Chemist Green be instructed
to analyze the fish and see whether or
not his conclusions were correct. Sev
eral citizens who had purchased some of
the shrimps in open market and who suf
fered for their indiscretion made the
neuter still more urgent, and the chem
ist was told to get to his work on the
analysis as soon as possible.
Professor Green will chop the shrimps
ir.to small pieces. He will put the tt.-h
through various machines of brass, glass
and iron; pass them through varied col
ored acids and alkalies, gradually dissipat
ing the fleshy substance and the bor.es
and aiming to save nothing except the
poisonous ptomaines, if such exist. The
operation will require a number of days
and the outcome will be watched with
interest. Up to the present time pto
maine poisoning has never been clearly
explained by scientists. Investigations
on the subject arein progress in all of the
medical institutions of this country and
Europe. It is now believed that pro
cesses of decomposition in animal mat
ter generate ptomaines of several hun
dred different kinds. The eating of de
cayed animals by fish would transfer the
ptomaines to them.
If it 111 found that the Butchertown
shrimps are deadly when dead, fishermen
will be warned to let them alone in the
future and will be advised to do their
angling in Oakland and Alameda waters,
where the shrimps are of unblemished
reputation.
The shrimp investigation will be the
first work undertaken in the new labor
atory just finished. The apparatus is of
the most complete character. In fact,
the laboratory is the finest west of Chi
cago. It will be possible to conduct the
most exhaustive inquiries Into various
food products sold in the market, with a
view to the detection of adulterations.
PERU MUST NOW PAY
MACCORD $40,000
Award of the Chief Justice of Canada
for the Imprisonment of the
Consular Agent.
WASHINGTON, Oct. 27.â The award of
the Chief Justice of Canada, to whose ar
bitration was submitted the claim of Vic
tor H. Maccord against the Government
of Peru for damages sustained by reason
of imprisonment during one of the revo
lutionary outbreaks there, has been re
ceived at the State Department. Mac
cord is awarded $40,000. The payment of
this amount will close a diplomatic con
troversy which has been in progress be
tween the United States and Peru for six
years, this Government having persist
ently, but fruitlessly, heretofore urged
reparation for Maccord.
The settlement provides that the money
must be paid to Maccord within six
months from the date of the award. Fees
to the Chief Justice of Canadaâ slooo by
the United States and $1000 by Peru
were paid before the terms of the award
were made known to the respective gov
ernments. The arbitrator was the Right
Hon. Samuel Henry Strong, P. C. Mac
cord was Consular agent of the United
States at Arequlpa, Peru, In 1885, and also
superintendent of a railroad. During a
revolution he was thrown Into prison and
his life threatened. His original claim
was for $200,000.
Fire at Fresno.
FRESNO. Oct. 'a.â Fire broke out at
about 10 o'clock this evening in the furni
ture store of W. F. McVey, and in a half
hour the place ami $12,000 worth of prop
erty were destroyed. The fire began in the
rear of the store, where considerable ex
celsior and other inflammable material
was stored, and spread rapidly. Had it
not beefk for the bursting of hose, how
ever, it is likely that some of the prop
erty would have been saved. Mcvey s
stock was valued at $10,000, and the insur
ance on it was $6000. The damage to the
building, which is owned by the Bradley
heirs, amounts to about $2000. Nothing
is known of the origin of the fire.

PORTO RICANS SLAP!
BY SPANISH SOLDIERS
Details of the Biot at Arrecibo Be-
tween Countrymen and the
Troops.
SAN JUAN, Porto Rico, October 27.â
Full details of the Arreclbo Incident of
Sunday, October 9, have been received
here, although the Spanish censor declines
to allow their publication in the local
newspapers. It appears that the trouble
began In a row between some soldiers of
the Alfonso XIII battalion and a num
ber of countrymen. The soldiers retreat
ed to the neighboring barracks and, af
ter arming themselves with Mauser rifles,
returned and tired upon the crowd, kill
ing four men and wounding many. Tiie
crowd, to the number of 200, secured ma
chetes and rushed through the streets,
the soldiers retreating to the barracks.
The people then carried their wounded
to the house of the British Consul, where
they found several United States officers
and soldiers, from whom they asked jus
tice.
When the wounded were cared for the
townspeople returned to the scene of the
conflict to destroy the houses of several
well-known Spaniards. Ultimately, how
ever, the persuasive word of cooler men,
coupled with the establishment of a vigi
lance committee, succeeded in restoring
order.
But only for a time. The same after
noon a party of forty Porto Ricans com
pletely destroyed the business house of a
well-known Spaniard. During the affair
an American was killed. The night was
not without its alarm. There was shoot
ing at the barracks, though no one was
hurt. Seventeen different tires in the sur
rounding country were counted from the
housetops of Arrecibp. The following day
most of the business houses remained
closed. That night twenty-four fires were
counted. On Tuesday most of the Spanish
business houses remained closed and
those that opened tht-ir doors were soon
forced to close. The military commander
at last issued an order directing that all
places of business be closed forthwith.

Or. Saturday next a meeting of the direc

"Little Giant." Hutchison and Mo-
Craney Address the Democrats
of Placerville.
PLACERVILLE, Oct. 27.â The largest
political meeting held here during the
campaign was addressed by James G.
Maguire to-night. After speaking for two
hours Judge Maguire was followed by H.
A. McCraney, union candidate for Clerk
of the Sunreme Court, and Edward I-.
Hutchison, candidate for Lieutenant Gov-
ernor.
WEBSTER DAVIS AT FRESNO.
FRESNO, Oct. 27.â Webster Davis. As
sistant Secretary of the Interior, spoke
in Armory Hall to-night to a large audi
ence in spite of the counter attractions
offered by Wallace's circus and the thea
ter. His speech was an eloquent one
and greatly enjoyed, the applause being
frequent and enthusiastic. As the speech
was drawing to a close the fire bells rang
and the people who were proof against
the temptation of the circus and the the
ater began to fidget in their seats and a
few left the hall. Mr. Davis quickly
took in the situation and wisely cut his
address tfhort.
The Arrest of Chin Noon.
SACRAMENTO, Oct. i'7.â Chief of Police
Dwyer this afternoon received a telephone
message from the San Francisco police
office to the effect that Detective Gibson
had gone to consult with the Chinese Con
sul concerning the arrest In this city last
night of Chin Noon on a charge of mur
der. The crime for which he was ar
rested was committed in San Francisco
last Monday night. Chin Noon was then
in this city, employed as a tailor. He
evidently knew, however, that the crime
was to be committed, us he went to see
Chief Dwyer that night to prove his
whereabouts. The Chinaman may be
taken to San Francisco.
Captured Cannon Coining.
WASHINGTON, Oct. 27.â Some of the
cannon captured at Santiago are now on
their way to the United States. These
pieces will be stored at Governor's Isl
and until disposed of by Congress.
Ontario Shaken.
OTTAWA, Ont., Oct. 27.â About 1 o'clock
this morning an earthquake shock passed
over the city. It was felt distinctly on
Sandy Hill and in the vicinity of Gilmour.
The duration was about ten seconds.
Orleans Goes to Brussels.
VIENNA, Oct. 27.â The Duke of Orleans
has started for Brussels.

UNITED LABOR
PARTY SHOWN
TO BE A FRAUD
Many Forgeries in Its
Petition.
NO PRIMARIES WERE HELD
PHELAHPS PLAN OF APPOINTING
DELEGATES FOLLOWED.
Attorney Garret McEnerney Calls W.
P. Grace a Mountebank and aii
Enemy to Society â A
Warm Time.
The Supreme Court did not listen yes
terday to the argument that was to have
been made on the writ of mandate grant
ed by that body on Wednesday to W. P.
Grace, of the sokcalled United Labor
party, against Registrar Biggy. Instead
the court called in Commissioner Chip
man to listen to testimony that indicated
fraud and forgery on a big scale on the
part of the managers of Grace's alleged
political body.
Garret McEnerney, for the respondent,
said in his opening statement that he
proposed to prove that most of the names
on Grace's petition were either forged or
otherwise fraudulent; that the party it
self was no more than a piece club and
a trap through which Grace seeks to get
money by false pretense from the candi
dates for Indorsing their nominations,
and that the money so secured he intend
ed to use for his own private purposes.
He called him a mountebank and an ene
my of society.
Though W. H. H. Hart, for the peti
tioner, argued that as Registrar Blggy's
duties were purely ministerial, he had no
right to go behind the face of the peti
tion to look for fraud. Chief Justice Beat
ty ordered that testimony be taken by
the Commissioner.
Registrar Blggy was the first witness
to take the stand. He testified that Grace
had presented his petition for tiling on
the evening of the 18th lnst, and that he
turned the papers over to his counsel on
the same evening, and that he had also
submitted them to the chairman of the
Grand Jury. "At that time," said Mr.
Biggy, "I was working on two petitions,
both purporting to be of the United Labor j
party. 1 set my deputies to work on the
Grace petition, aiid we found many errors
and apparent forgeries. I so informed
Mr. Grace on the evening of the 2uth. In
ISOO names we found 903 that were not on
the register and 22S that were forged. We
found only 467 in the whole lot that were
genuine."
Chairman Grace followed Mr. Biggy to
the stand. Grace said he was a carpen
ter, but that It had been so long since he
had worked that he had forgotten the
Lapses of memory characterized
his testimony from start to finish. He
could not remember the date of the con
vention, which he thought his party had
held, but thought that it was some time I
last month. Nor could he recall the
names of any of the fifty or sixty mem
bers he thought were present. When
asked how his delegates had been selected
Grace said that he had pursued the plan
of Phelan and his committee of one hun
dred jobchasers and had appointed them.
"You held no primaries?"' said Mr. Mc-
Enerney, in surprise. "No," was the re
ply, "we followed the example of Mayor
Phelan's committee and appointed our
own men whom we knew would stand in
with us."
Grace acknowledged that during the
election of 1596 he had pursued the same
plan he is working now, and at that
time the Republican committee had prom
ised him $250, which he never received.
Mr. McEnerney attempted to pinhimdown
to a statement of how much he had been
promised on the present election and fin
ally elicited the admission that about $500
had been promised. "But," said Grace,
"I'd take fifty per cent if they would pay
it to me down.
Commissioner Chlpman announced that
he had heard enough of that sort of e%'i
dence to acquaint him with the nature of |
Grace's party, and one of Grace's solici
tors, Frank Buckley, was placed on the
stand. His statement was short but im
portant. He swore he had forged, at the
request of Grace, a number of the signa
tures that appeared as bona fide on the
petition and he read the names he had
so forged. Commissioner Chipman shut
out further testimony along that line by
continuing the matter to 10 o'clock thi3
morning, when other witnesses to testify
to forgeries will be introduced.
SARAH B. COOPER'S ESTATE.
The Administratrix Files a Report
and Asks That It Be Dis
tributed.
Harriet E. Skilton, administratrix of
the will of the late Sarah B. Cooper,
filed a report and petition for settlement
of account and final distribution of the es
tate. In the report the administratrix
says she has sold the legacy of $,~<wi
under the last will of Miranda W. Lux
and applied the proceeds to the assets
of the estate. Since the date of her ap
pointment the administratrix avers that
$4536 24 in money has been received by
her; she has paid all outstanding- ac
counts and brought about a complete set
tlement.
The administratrix then asks that the
balance of the estate of Sarah B. Cooper
be distributed to Irene W. Rawlings, ad
ministratrix of the estate of Harriet
Cooper, that the provisions of her will,
she being the sole devisee of her moth
er's estate, may be carried out.
ROWING MEN ENTERTAIN.
Ariel Club Reception and Dance at
Union Square Hall Last Night.
The stage and galleries of Union Square
Hall were gracefully draped last night
with festoons of blue and yellow cheese
cloth in honor of the Ariel Rowing Club's
annual entertainment and dance. After
a potpourri of popular airs had been ac
ceptably rendered by Temple's orchestra,
J. R. Bockman made a few introductory
remarks, which were received with a de
gree of appreciation that clearly proved
his popularity. The well-kno#vn ama
teur singer, Ben Tarbox, sang "The
Champion of the King." and for an en
core gave an anvil song. Messrs. Morey
and McGorey entertained the audience
with selections on the banjo, and Miss

20 TIMES
My little boy broke with an itching rash.
I tried three doctors and medical college but
he kept getting worse. There teas not one
: squareinch of skinonhiswholebody unaffected.
He was one mass of sores, and the stench was
frightful. In remoring the bandages they
would take the skin with them, and the poor
child's screams were heart-breaking. After
the second application of Cuticttbjl (oint-
ment) I saw signs of improvement, and the
sores to dry up. His skin peeled off twenty
times, but now he is entirely cured.

NATIVE SONS AT THE TIVOLI.
Successful Presentation of Lecocq's
"Girofle-Girofla" for the Benefit
of Olympus Parlor.
The Tivoli Opera-house was crowded
last night, chiefly with Native Sons and
Native Daughters, the occasion being a
benefit entertainment for Olympus Parlor
of the Native Sons. \V. A. Deane, Re
publican candidate for County Clerk, had
a box, and his opponent, John A. Brown,
and F. J. Burke, candidate for Justice of
the Peace, occupied another box. The
other two boxes were occupied by F. J.
Muraskv, Democratic candidate for Su
perior Judge, and A. Berthier, candidate
for Recorder.
Lecocq's celebrated comic opera, "Giro
fle-Girona," was the attraction, and was
pleasingly rendered. Elvla Crox assumed
the title role, and was twice encored lor
her capital rendition of <r See How it
Sparkles."
The officers of Olympus Parlor are: A.
Mocker, junior pastpresident; O. C. Pratt,
president; James A. Simpson, first vice I
president; M. A. Devlne, second vice presi
dent, and C. L. Mitchell, third vice presi
dent. The committee in charge of the en
tertainment last night was composed of
W. Phelps, John T. Cosgrove, J. B. Mar
tin, W. Cane, A. Mocker and H. F. Mul
creavy- ____^_______
DEATH CLAIMS A LITTLE
CHILD AT ITS PLAY
HAROLD HAVER SUCCUMBS TO
CARBOLIC POISONING.
While His Mother Slept He Found a
Bottle of the Acid and Swal
lowed Its Contents.
Harold Haver, the three-year-old son
of Philip I. Haver, a contractor living at
3681 Twenty-fourth street, died yesterday
afternoon from drinking a glass of car
bolic acid which he found in a closet.
The mother of the boy has been ill and
while she was lying on a sofa asleep the
little fellow wandered into the kitchen.
In an adjoining- closet, which contained
a number of bottles of medicine, he found
a bottle filled with carbolic acid. Pour
j ing a large portion into a glass he drank
I it. Screaming with pain he ran to his
mother, who took him in her arms to a
near-by drug store. A physician was
summoned hastily, but the child died de
spite his efforts to save the little life.
The Coroner's office was notified, and
Deputy J. G. Tyrrell went on the case.
As the father waived an inquest the body
was not taken to the Morgue.
THE SPORT OF KINGS.
High Class Dogs That Will Speed
With the Wind at Ingle
side Park.
Great sport will certainly be in order
for the lovers of coursing at Ingleside
Park on Saturday and Sunday. The dogs
that figure in the drawing which was held
last evening are the cream of the ken
nels of this city and State. Among the
saplings are many new dogs which from
appearances should certainly give good
accounts or themselves. The match race
between Mayflower and Rock Island Boy
is all the talk in coursing circles and
will doubtless attract sport lovers from
many parts of the State. The drawing
for the open and sapling stakes resulted
as follows:
